The Mujassimah's Proof for Corporeality
Al-Jurjani presents the three proofs of the Mujassimah for corporeality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the three arguments the Mujassimah used from intuition, self-subsistence, and scripture to claim that Allah must be a body.

Core Idea
Scriptural literalists used flawed premises to force a bodily interpretation of Allah.

Before refuting, we must understand the opposing argument. The Mujassimah relied on three proofs: rational intuition, a principle about self-subsistence, and selective scriptural evidence. Al-Jurjani presents them here to then dismantle them.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice that Al-Jurjani presents these proofs as part of the opponent's case.
2
Identify the three sub-arguments
The passage contains three distinct proofs. Find each one and notice how each is introduced with 'أن' or 'أيضاً'.
3
Examine the rational intuition claim
The first proof claims that intuition divides existents into either extended or in extended. Ask yourself: is this division exhaustive for the divine?
This is a key point the answer will challenge.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحجتهم ما تقدم من أن كل موجود فهو إما متحيز أو حال في المتحيز كما تشهد به البديهة. والثاني مما لا يتصور في حقه تعالى والأول هو الجسم وأيضاً كل قائم بنفسه جسم وأيضاً الآيات والأحاديث دالة على كونه جسماً ( والجواب ) بإسنادهما إلى نفس الإشارة المذكورة كما هو الظاهر من العبارة، ففيه أنها إنما تدل على ذلك لو كان السؤال بأين الإله وأما إذا كان باين الله تعالى كما الاستدلال فالدلالة المذكور في المذكورة محل بحث لأن الله إسم خاص لذاته تعالى لم يطلق على غيره لا في الجاهلية ولا الإسلام كما صرحوا به فمن أين يعلم من تلك الإشارة أنها ليست وثنية وإنها تريد أنه تعالى خالق السماء وليست على معتقد كفار العرب من كونه تعالى في السماء على ما ذكره القاضي وغيره في تفسير قوله تعالى : أأمنتم من في السماء [ تبارك : ۱۷] ، من جواز كون الآية على زعم العرب فإنهم يزعمون أن الله تعالى في السماء تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً على أنه يلزم مما ذكر عدم مطابقة جواب الخرساء للسؤال بأين الله والقصد إلى خلاف ما يترقب السامع من الجارية الأمية بعيد جداً، ولا رباب المكاشفة فيه وفي أمثاله توجيه ذكر في مفتاح الغيب وغيره والله أعلم .
And their proof is what preceded: that every existent is either extended or inhere in an extended, as the intuition testifies. The second is inconceivable in His right, and the first is the body. Also, every self-subsistent is a body. Also, the verses and hadiths indicate that He is a body. (And the answer:) By attributing both [the narration and the hadith] to the same mentioned indication, as is apparent from the expression. It contains that they only indicate that if the question were 'where is the god?' But if it is 'where is Allah' as in the proof, then the mentioned indication in the mentioned [place] is debatable, because 'Allah' is a proper name for His essence, not applied to other than Him, neither in the Jahiliyyah nor in Islam as they stated. So how is it known from that indication that it is not pagan, and that it intends that He is the Creator of the heaven, and not upon the belief of the Arab disbelievers that He is in the heaven, as al-Qadi and others mentioned in the interpretation of His saying: 'Do you feel secure from He who is in the heaven' (Surah al-Mulk: 17) – regarding the possibility that the verse is according to the claim of the Arabs, for they claim that Allah is in the heaven, exalted above that greatly. Also, what was mentioned necessitates the lack of correspondence of the mute girl's answer to the question 'where is Allah', and the intent contrary to what the listener expects from the unlettered slave girl is very far. And there is no [reliance on] unveiling in this and its likes; a direction has been mentioned in Miftah al-Ghayb and others. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of the Mujassimah and its answer. The objection is that every existent is either extended or in an extended; the second is impossible for Allah, so He must be the first (body). Also, all self-subsistent are bodies, and the Quran and hadith indicate a body. The answer refutes the scriptural evidence: the hadith of the slave girl uses the proper name 'Allah', not the generic 'god', so the indication is not general. Moreover, the Arabs of Jahiliyyah believed Allah was in heaven, so interpreting such texts literally would adopt their false belief. The answer also notes that the slave girl's answer 'in the heaven' does not match the question 'where is Allah' if taken literally; rather, she meant that Allah is the Creator of the heaven, as was the likely intent of the simple girl. The passage concludes that such matters are not based on mystical unveiling but on interpretation.

Key Terms
حجتهم
بديهة
متحيز
حال
قائم بنفسه

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following is NOT one of the three proofs presented by the Mujassimah?
A) All existents are either bodies or attributes
B) Every self-subsistent is a body
C) Scriptural texts indicate corporeality
D) The hadith of the slave girl

Key Takeaway
The Mujassimah built their case on a rational principle, a metaphysical claim, and scriptural literalism, all of which will be refuted.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read that the Mujassimah used rational intuition to claim that Allah must be a body, do you ever catch yourself imagining Allah in some form or shape despite knowing He is beyond that? What would it take for your heart to truly feel His incomparability in every moment of worship?
?
The Mujassimah relied on a flawed premise that every self-subsistent is a body. Are there any beliefs about Allah that you hold simply because they are common or feel intuitive, without verifying them against revelation and sound reason? What would it cost you to examine them honestly?
?
If you were to strip away every mental image of Allah—every throne, every hand, every direction—would your connection to Him become more intimate or more distant? What are you willing to let go of in order to know Allah as He truly is?

Al-Jurjani's Refutation of the Scriptural Evidence
Al-Jurjani answers the Mujassimah's scriptural proof using the hadith of the slave girl.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Jurjani refutes the scriptural proof by analyzing the hadith of the slave girl and the divine name 'Allah'.

Core Idea
The hadith's use of 'Allah' as a proper name prevents it from being a generic proof of corporeality.

The answer to the Mujassimah focuses on their strongest evidence: the hadith of the slave girl. Al-Jurjani points out that the question used the proper name 'Allah', not the generic 'god', so the indication is not general. He also notes that pagan Arabs believed Allah was in heaven, so such texts must be interpreted accordingly.

Guided Path
1
Read the answer passage
Read the Arabic passage from the answer till the end. Then read the translation. Notice the key arguments.
2
Understand the proper name argument
Al-Jurjani argues that because the question used 'Allah', a unique proper name, the indication of 'where' does not automatically imply location. Ask yourself why this distinction matters.
This is the core of the refutation.
3
Consider the pagan Arab belief
The passage mentions that Arab disbelievers also believed Allah was in heaven. How does that weaken the literal interpretation?
Al-Jurjani uses this to show the text could be accommodating their misunderstanding.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وحجتهم ما تقدم من أن كل موجود فهو إما متحيز أو حال في المتحيز كما تشهد به البديهة. والثاني مما لا يتصور في حقه تعالى والأول هو الجسم وأيضاً كل قائم بنفسه جسم وأيضاً الآيات والأحاديث دالة على كونه جسماً ( والجواب ) بإسنادهما إلى نفس الإشارة المذكورة كما هو الظاهر من العبارة، ففيه أنها إنما تدل على ذلك لو كان السؤال بأين الإله وأما إذا كان باين الله تعالى كما الاستدلال فالدلالة المذكور في المذكورة محل بحث لأن الله إسم خاص لذاته تعالى لم يطلق على غيره لا في الجاهلية ولا الإسلام كما صرحوا به فمن أين يعلم من تلك الإشارة أنها ليست وثنية وإنها تريد أنه تعالى خالق السماء وليست على معتقد كفار العرب من كونه تعالى في السماء على ما ذكره القاضي وغيره في تفسير قوله تعالى : أأمنتم من في السماء [ تبارك : ۱۷] ، من جواز كون الآية على زعم العرب فإنهم يزعمون أن الله تعالى في السماء تعالى عن ذلك علواً كبيراً على أنه يلزم مما ذكر عدم مطابقة جواب الخرساء للسؤال بأين الله والقصد إلى خلاف ما يترقب السامع من الجارية الأمية بعيد جداً، ولا رباب المكاشفة فيه وفي أمثاله توجيه ذكر في مفتاح الغيب وغيره والله أعلم .

Also, every self-subsistent is a body. Also, the verses and hadiths indicate that He is a body. (And the answer:) By attributing both [the narration and the hadith] to the same mentioned indication, as is apparent from the expression. It contains that they only indicate that if the question were 'where is the god?' But if it is 'where is Allah' as in the proof, then the mentioned indication in the mentioned [place] is debatable, because 'Allah' is a proper name for His essence, not applied to other than Him, neither in the Jahiliyyah nor in Islam as they stated. So how is it known from that indication that it is not pagan, and that it intends that He is the Creator of the heaven, and not upon the belief of the Arab disbelievers that He is in the heaven, as al-Qadi and others mentioned in the interpretation of His saying: 'Do you feel secure from He who is in the heaven' (Surah al-Mulk: 17) – regarding the possibility that the verse is according to the claim of the Arabs, for they claim that Allah is in the heaven, exalted above that greatly. Also, what was mentioned necessitates the lack of correspondence of the mute girl's answer to the question 'where is Allah', and the intent contrary to what the listener expects from the unlettered slave girl is very far. And there is no [reliance on] unveiling in this and its likes; a direction has been mentioned in Miftah al-Ghayb and others. And Allah knows best.
Source pages p. 29

Commentary
This passage presents the objection of the Mujassimah and its answer. The objection is that every existent is either extended or in an extended; the second is impossible for Allah, so He must be the first (body). Also, all self-subsistent are bodies, and the Quran and hadith indicate a body. According to Al-Jurjani, why does the hadith of the slave girl not prove that Allah is spatially located?
A) Because the girl was mute and her answer is unreliable

C) Because the hadith is weak

Key Takeaway

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?

Are you willing to submit your immediate understanding to sound scholarship, even if it feels counterintuitive? What concrete step will you take to train yourself in proper interpretation?
?
The proper name 'Allah' is unique and does not imply location. How does reflecting on the uniqueness of Allah's name change the way you think about His relationship to creation? Will you commit to invoking His name with greater awe and awareness of His transcendence?

The Ninth Proof: Past Tense Implies Temporality
Presents the ninth proof: past tense verbs in revelation indicate temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why Allah's use of the past tense in the Quran indicates that the Quran cannot be eternal.

Core Idea
Past tense reports cannot apply to an eternal being without falsehood.

The ninth proof from Muslim theologians argues that the Quran's past tense verbs, like 'We sent it down', demonstrate its createdness. Because there is no 'past' in pre-eternity, these statements would be false if the Quran were eternal. This lesson examines the logic behind this argument and a subtle commentary on its wording.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation, to grasp the argument in the author's own words.
Notice the use of 'We sent it down' and 'We sent' as examples.
2
Understand the core logical problem
The proof argues that past tense requires a time before the act, which is impossible in pre-eternity (azal). If the Quran were eternal, these reports would be false.
Think about why 'past' cannot exist in relation to pre-eternity.
3
Note the commentator's clarification
The commentator suggests that the phrase 'no sending in pre-eternity' might involve ellipsis, meaning 'no sending before pre-eternity', to maintain consistency.
This shows how scholars carefully interpret theological texts.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : أنه تعالى أخبر بلفظ الماضي نحو إنا أنزلناه ، إنا أرسلنا ) ولا شك أنه لا نزال ولا إرسال في الأزل، فلو كان كلامه قديماً لكان كذباً لأنه إخبار بالوقوع في ما الماضي ولا يتصور هو ماض بالقياس إلى الأزل . العاشر : النسخ حق بإجماع الأمة ووقع في القرآن وهو ( رفع ) أو انتهاء ( و ) لا شيء منهما يتصور في القديم لأن ( ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه . قوله: (ولا إرسال في الأزل فضلاً عن الإرسال فيما قبل الأزل ويمكن أن يصار أيضاً إلى الحذف، أي لا إرسال فيما قبل الأزل فحينئذ يتلاءم مع سابق الكلام ولاحقه فتدبر. قوله: (ولا شيء منهما يتصور في القديم للحنابلة أن يقولوا : معنى نسخ القرآن رفع حكمه لا ذاته فلا يلزم حدوث ذاته . والإمام الرازي جعل هذين الوجهين في الأربعين من الأدلة المعقولة، والحق ما اختاره المصنف. قوله: (وألحق ما اختاره المصنف) لأن جميع مقدماته القريبة ليست عقلية.
The ninth: That He, exalted, informed with the past tense, such as 'Indeed We sent it down' and 'We sent', and there is no doubt that there is no sending down nor sending in pre-eternity (azal). Thus if His speech were eternal, it would be a lie, because it is an informing of occurrence in the past, and it is not conceivable that it is past in relation to pre-eternity. The tenth: Abrogation (naskh) is established by consensus of the community and occurs in the Quran, and it is either removal or termination, and neither of them is conceivable in the eternal, because 'what has been proven eternal is impossible of non-existence.' The saying: 'There is no sending in pre-eternity, let alone sending before pre-eternity, and it is also possible to resort to ellipsis, i.e., no sending before pre-eternity; then it will be consistent with the preceding and following speech, so reflect.' The saying: 'Neither of them is conceivable in the eternal' – the Hanbalis may say: The meaning of abrogation of the Quran is the removal of its ruling, not its essence, so the temporality of its essence is not necessitated. And Imam al-Razi placed these two points among forty rational proofs, but the truth is what the author chose. The saying: 'And the truth is what the author chose' because all its immediate premises are not rational.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents two additional proofs for the createdness of the Quran. The ninth proof: Allah uses past tense verbs like 'We sent down' (anzalna) to describe revelation. Since there is no revelation or sending in pre-eternity (azal), if the Quran were eternal, such reports would be false, because they claim an event occurred in the past relative to pre-eternity, which is impossible. Therefore, the Quran must be temporal. The commentary suggests that perhaps the phrase means that there is no sending before pre-eternity, and the wording might involve ellipsis. The tenth proof: Abrogation (naskh) occurs in the Quran, and abrogation is either removal or termination. Both are inconceivable for an eternal entity because what is eternal cannot cease to exist. Thus the Quran must be temporal. The commentary notes that Hanbalis argue that abrogation only removes the ruling, not the essence, so it does not imply temporality. The text then states that Imam al-Razi counted these two among rational proofs, but the author (of al-Mawaqif) chose the truth that these proofs are not purely rational (they are scriptural).

Key Terms
الأزل
الماضي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think that past tense verbs can be applied to eternal acts metaphorically.
Correction: The argument relies on the literal meaning of past tense, which entails a time before the moment of speech, impossible for an eternal being.

Checkpoint
Why does the ninth proof argue that the Quran cannot be eternal?
A) Because the Quran was revealed in Arabic.
B) Because Allah uses past tense verbs for revelation, but there is no revelation in pre-eternity, so if the Quran were eternal, these reports would be false.
C) Because the Quran is a miracle.
D) Because the Quran is composed of chapters.

Key Takeaway
The use of past tense in revelation demonstrates that the Quran is created, because eternal speech cannot truthfully refer to a past event.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Quran's past tense verbs are evidence that it is created and thus entered time, how does that change the way you receive its commands? Are you treating them as timely instructions for today or as abstract eternal truths?
?
The proof shows that aeternal speech cannot truthfully report a past event. When you read 'We sent it down', do you feel the weight of that moment in history? Or do you read over it as a mere phrase? What would it take for you to truly believe that the Quran entered human time?
?
This lesson argues that the literal meaning of past tense verbs is sufficient to demonstrate creation. Are you tempted to evade the force of this argument by appealing to metaphor or allegory? Examine your heart: are you resisting the createdness of the Quran because it unsettles your assumptions about divine speech?

The Tenth Proof: Abrogation Implies Change
Presents the tenth proof: abrogation in the Quran demonstrates temporality.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the phenomenon of abrogation in the Quran indicates that the Quran cannot be eternal and must be created.

Core Idea
Abrogation involves removal or termination, both impossible for an eternal entity.

The tenth proof appeals to the well-known fact of abrogation (naskh) in the Quran. Abrogation is either removal or termination, but an eternal thing cannot undergo such changes. Therefore, the Quran must be temporal. This lesson also covers the Hanbali response and the author's classification of this proof.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once, then its translation, to grasp the argument in the author's own words.
Notice the definition of abrogation as removal (رفع) or termination (انتهاء).
2
Identify the two forms of abrogation and why they contradict eternality
Abrogation means either removal or termination. Both imply change or cessation, which cannot happen to something that is eternal.
Consider the principle: 'what has been proven eternal is impossible of non-existence.'
3
Consider the Hanbali objection and the author's conclusion
Hanbalis argue that abrogation only affects the ruling, not the essence of the Quran. The author states that this proof is not purely rational but scriptural.
Think about why the author considers the premises not purely rational.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
التاسع : أنه تعالى أخبر بلفظ الماضي نحو إنا أنزلناه ، إنا أرسلنا ) ولا شك أنه لا نزال ولا إرسال في الأزل، فلو كان كلامه قديماً لكان كذباً لأنه إخبار بالوقوع في ما الماضي ولا يتصور هو ماض بالقياس إلى الأزل . العاشر : النسخ حق بإجماع الأمة ووقع في القرآن وهو ( رفع ) أو انتهاء ( و ) لا شيء منهما يتصور في القديم لأن ( ما ثبت قدمه امتنع عدمه . قوله: (ولا إرسال في الأزل فضلاً عن الإرسال فيما قبل الأزل ويمكن أن يصار أيضاً إلى الحذف، أي لا إرسال فيما قبل الأزل فحينئذ يتلاءم مع سابق الكلام ولاحقه فتدبر. قوله: (ولا شيء منهما يتصور في القديم للحنابلة أن يقولوا : معنى نسخ القرآن رفع حكمه لا ذاته فلا يلزم حدوث ذاته . والإمام الرازي جعل هذين الوجهين في الأربعين من الأدلة المعقولة، والحق ما اختاره المصنف. قوله: (وألحق ما اختاره المصنف) لأن جميع مقدماته القريبة ليست عقلية.
The ninth: That He, exalted, informed with the past tense, such as 'Indeed We sent it down' and 'We sent', and there is no doubt that there is no sending down nor sending in pre-eternity (azal). Thus if His speech were eternal, it would be a lie, because it is an informing of occurrence in the past, and it is not conceivable that it is past in relation to pre-eternity. The tenth: Abrogation (naskh) is established by consensus of the community and occurs in the Quran, and it is either removal or termination, and neither of them is conceivable in the eternal, because 'what has been proven eternal is impossible of non-existence.' The saying: 'There is no sending in pre-eternity, let alone sending before pre-eternity, and it is also possible to resort to ellipsis, i.e., no sending before pre-eternity; then it will be consistent with the preceding and following speech, so reflect.' The saying: 'Neither of them is conceivable in the eternal' – the Hanbalis may say: The meaning of abrogation of the Quran is the removal of its ruling, not its essence, so the temporality of its essence is not necessitated. And Imam al-Razi placed these two points among forty rational proofs, but the truth is what the author chose. The saying: 'And the truth is what the author chose' because all its immediate premises are not rational.
Source pages p. 111

Commentary
This passage presents two additional proofs for the createdness of the Quran. The ninth proof: Allah uses past tense verbs like 'We sent down' (anzalna) to describe revelation. Since there is no revelation or sending in pre-eternity (azal), if the Quran were eternal, such reports would be false, because they claim an event occurred in the past relative to pre-eternity, which is impossible. Therefore, the Quran must be temporal. The commentary suggests that perhaps the phrase means that there is no sending before pre-eternity, and the wording might involve ellipsis. The tenth proof: Abrogation (naskh) occurs in the Quran, and abrogation is either removal or termination. Both are inconceivable for an eternal entity because what is eternal cannot cease to exist. Thus the Quran must be temporal. The commentary notes that Hanbalis argue that abrogation only removes the ruling, not the essence, so it does not imply temporality. The text then states that Imam al-Razi counted these two among rational proofs, but the author (of al-Mawaqif) chose the truth that these proofs are not purely rational (they are scriptural).

Key Terms
النسخ
الرفع
الانتهاء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some think abrogation proves the Quran changes in its essence.
Correction: The Hanbali response clarifies that abrogation only changes the ruling, not the Quranic text or its eternal essence (if one holds it to be eternal).

Checkpoint
Why does abrogation imply the Quran is not eternal?
A) Because abrogation involves removal or termination, both impossible for an eternal entity.
B) Because abrogation shows Allah changed His mind.
C) Because abrogation is a new revelation.
D) Because abrogation only applies to non-Quranic texts.

Key Takeaway
Abrogation in the Quran, whether seen as removal or termination, cannot be reconciled with an eternal Quran, indicating its createdness.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If the Quran were eternal and uncreated, how would you reconcile that with the clear presence of abrogated verses? Does this challenge your understanding of God's unchanging will?
?
When you read an abrogated verse, do you treat it with the same reverence as a current one? How does knowing that some rulings have been superseded affect your trust in the Quran's finality?
?
The proof argues that change implies temporality. Is there an area of your faith where you resist change, thinking it implies weakness? What would it mean to accept that God's guidance is dynamic and responsive, not static?

The First Scriptural Doubt: The Verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار'
Presentation of the first scriptural doubt against vision of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first scriptural doubt against the vision of God, based on the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار', and the reasoning that equates idrak with ru'ya and uses the lām of genus to argue that God cannot be seen at any time, including the Hereafter.

Core Idea
The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' is the strongest scriptural argument against seeing God in the afterlife.

Before addressing the refutation of this doubt, we must first understand its precise formulation. The doubt centers on the equation of idrak (perception) with ru'ya (vision) and the claim that the verse's unrestricted negation implies that God cannot be seen at any time, including the Hereafter. This passage lays out the core reasoning of the objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic passage and translation
Read the Arabic text once through, then the literal translation. Notice the flow: it begins by stating there are four scriptural doubts, then presents the first one.
The textual note at the end is a separate remark about manuscript variants.
2
Identify the core equation
The doubt equates idrak (perception) with ru'ya (vision). The text says 'والإدراك المضاف إلى الإبصار إنما هو الرؤية' — perception attributed to sight is nothing other than vision.
This is crucial because the verse denies idrak, so it is taken to deny ru'ya.
3
Understand the universality argument
The verse says 'لا تدركه الأبصار' with lām of genus, which in Arabic implies hyperbole and universality. The argument claims that this negates perception by all eyes in all times, including the Hereafter. The text gives an analogy: 'your saying So-and-so is perceived by eyes does not convey time generality, so its opposite must convey it.'
This is the logical move to extend the negation to the afterlife.
4
Note the additional point about self-praise
God is described as taking pride (تمددح) in not being seen, as He mentions it among praises. This reinforces the idea that not being seen is a perfection, implying it is impossible for God to be seen.
5
Review the textual note
The final sentence clarifies a manuscript issue: some copies mistakenly thought the second doubt comprised three separate doubts, but the correct enumeration is that the third and fourth are distinct.
This note does not affect the substance of the doubt.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( وأما ) الشبه ( السمعية فأربع ) لاست كما وقع في بعض النسخ ( الأولى : قوله تعالى : ولا تدركه الأبصار ﴾ [الأنعام : ۱۰۳] ، والإدراك المضاف إلى الإبصار إنما هو الرؤية ) فمعنى قولك أدركته ببصري معنى رأيته لا فرق إلا في اللفظ ( أو هما ) أمران ( متلازمان لا يصح نفي أحدهما مع إثبات الآخر فلا يجوز رؤيته وما أدركته ببصري ولا عكسه ( فالآية نفت أن تراه الأبصار ذلك يتناول جميع الأبصار) بواسطة اللام الجنسبة في مقام المبالغة ( في جميع الأوقات ( لأن قولك : فلان تدركه الأبصار لا يفيد عموم الأوقات فلا بد أن يفيده ما يقابله فلا يراه شيء من الأبصار لا في الدنيا ولا في الآخرة لما ذكرنا ) ولأنه تعالى تمدح بكونه لا يرى فإنه ذكره في اثناء المدائح. قوله : (كما وقع في بعض النسخ لعل منشأ الغلط توهم أن الشبهة الثانية المقرونة بثلاث آيات ثلاث شبه وليس كذلك كما دل عليه قوله : الثالثة الرابعة .
(As for the scriptural doubts, they are four) as it occurred in some copies. (The first: His saying, exalted: 'Vision does not perceive Him' (Q. 6:103), and perception attributed to sight is nothing other than vision) so the meaning of your saying 'I perceived him with my sight' is the meaning of 'I saw him'—no difference except in wording. (Or they are two) matters (that are inseparable, so it is not sound to negate one while affirming the other, so it is not permissible to see Him and not have perceived Him with my sight, nor the reverse.) So the verse negated that eyes see Him, and that encompasses all eyes) by means of the lām of genus in a context of hyperbole (in all times, because your saying 'So-and-so is perceived by eyes' does not convey generality of times, so its opposite must convey it. Thus no eye will see Him, neither in this world nor in the Hereafter, due to what we mentioned) and because He, exalted, prides Himself on not being seen, for He mentioned it among the praises. His statement: (as it occurred in some copies: perhaps the source of the error is the delusion that the second doubt, accompanied by three verses, is three doubts, but it is not so, as is indicated by his saying: the third, the fourth.)
Source pages p. 155

Commentary
This passage presents the first scriptural doubt against the vision of God, based on the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' (Vision does not perceive Him). The doubt argues that idrāk (perception) by sight is identical to vision, and the verse denies perception by all eyes at all times, including the Hereafter, and God prides Himself on not being seen. The author then adds a textual note clarifying that a misreading of the manuscript led some to think the second doubt comprised three separate doubts, but the correct enumeration is that the third and fourth are distinct.

Key Terms
شبهة سمعية
لا تدركه الأبصار
إدراك
رؤية
لام الجنس
عموم الأوقات

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' definitively proves that God cannot be seen in the Hereafter.
Correction: While the doubt uses this verse to argue against vision in the afterlife, Ash'ari theologians interpret the verse as referring to this world only, not negating vision in the Hereafter.

Checkpoint
What is the basis for the objection that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' proves God cannot be seen in the Hereafter?
A) The verse uses the lām of genus indicating hyperbole
B) The verse denies vision at all times, including the Hereafter
C) The verse was revealed in Medina
D) The verse mentions both sight and hearing

Key Takeaway
The first scriptural doubt argues that the verse 'لا تدركه الأبصار' denies vision of God in both this world and the Hereafter by equating perception with sight and using the lām of genus to assert universality.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
God prides Himself on being unseen. Are you willing to love and worship a God you will never see with your eyes—or does your heart secretly demand a visible encounter?
?
If the strongest scriptural doubt against seeing God is answered by the Ash'aris by restricting it to this world, what would it take for you to be content with God's wisdom whether He shows Himself or not?

Second Auditory Doubt: Verses Condemning the Request for Vision
The commentator presents and answers the objection from three verses condemning requests for vision.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the Qur'anic condemnation of requests to see God is reconciled with the possibility of beatific vision.

Core Idea
Condemnation of requests to see God was for insolence, not for the request itself.

The second auditory doubt uses three Qur'anic verses where God condemns those who ask to see Him. The critic argues this proves vision is impossible. But the commentator shows that the condemnation was due to obstinacy, not the request per se. We'll examine the verses and the response to see how this distinction resolves the doubt.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice how the commentator presents the objection and then gives a concise answer.
2
Identify the three verses
The passage cites three verses from the Qur'an. Find each one and note the specific complaints and punishments mentioned.
These are from Al-Furqan, Al-Baqara, and An-Nisa.
3
Understand the objection
The objector argues that since God condemns the request to see Him, vision must be impossible. Ask yourself: what is the logic behind this?
The key term is 'استعظام' (considering it great/condemning).
4
Grasp the answer about obstinacy
The commentator answers that the condemnation was for insolence and obstinacy, not the request itself. See how this is supported by the parallel condemnation of requesting angels.
This is the core of the lesson: distinguishing the manner of request from its content.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الثانية : ( من الشبه السمعية ( أنه تعالى ما ذكر سؤال الرؤية ) في موضع من كتابه ( إلا وقد استعظمه وذلك في ثلاث آيات الأولى : وقال الذين لا يرجون لقاءنا لولا أنزل علينا الملائكة أو نرى ربنا لقد استكبروا في أنفسهم وعتوا عتوا كبيراً ﴾ [الفرقان : ۲۱ ] ، ولو كانت الرؤية ممكنة لما كان طالبها عاتياً أي مجاوزاً للحد ( مستكبراً ) رافعاً نفسه إلى مرتبة لا يليق بها بل كان ذلك نازلاً منزلة طلب سائر المعجزات)، الآية الثانية : وإذ قلتم يا موسى لن نؤمن لك حتى نرى الله جهرة ﴾ [البقرة : ٥٥]، أي عياناً فأخذتكم الصاعقة وأنتم تنظرون ﴾ [الذاريات : ٤٤] ، ولو أمكنت الرؤية لما عاقبهم بسؤالها في الحال. الآية الثالثة يسألك أهل الكتاب أن تنزل عليهم كتاباً السماء فقد سألوا موسى من الله جهرة فأخذتهم الصاعقة بظلمهم ﴾ [النساء :١٥٣]، سمى ( ظلماً وجازاهم به في الحال ) بأخذ الصاعقة ( ولو جاز ) كونه مرئياً ( لكان سؤالهم) هذا ( سؤالاً لمعجزة زائدة ) ولم يكن ظلماً ولا سبباً للعقاب ( والجواب أن الاستعظام إنما كان لطلبهم الرؤية تعنتاً وعناداً ولهذا استعظم إنزال الملائكة ) في الآية الأولى
The second of the auditory doubts: that God Almighty never mentioned the request for vision in a place of His book except that He considered it great, and that is in three verses: the first: 'And those who do not expect to meet Us say, "Why are not angels sent down to us, or do we see our Lord?" They have certainly become arrogant within themselves and have become insolent with great insolence.' [Al-Furqan: 21]. If vision were possible, the one requesting it would not be insolent, i.e., exceeding the limit, arrogant, raising himself to a rank not befitting him; rather, that would be comparable to requesting other miracles. The second verse: 'And when you said, "O Moses, we will never believe you until we see God outright," so the thunderbolt took you while you were looking.' [Al-Baqara: 55], i.e., openly. 'So the thunderbolt seized you while you were looking' [Adh-Dhariyat: 44]. If vision were possible, He would not have punished them for requesting it immediately. The third verse: 'The People of the Scripture ask you to bring down to them a book from the heaven. They asked Moses for what is greater than that, and they said, "Show us God outright," so the thunderbolt seized them for their wrongdoing.' [An-Nisa: 153]. He called it wrongdoing and punished them for it immediately with the thunderbolt. If it were possible for Him to be seen, their request would be a request for an additional miracle, and it would not be wrongdoing nor a cause for punishment. The answer: that the considering it great was only for their request of vision out of obstinacy and insolence, and for this reason He considered the sending down of angels as great in the first verse.
Source pages p. 158

Commentary
This passage presents the second auditory doubt from those who deny the possibility of seeing God on the grounds that the Qur'an condemns those who ask to see God. Three verses are cited: the request of the disbelievers in Surah Al-Furqan, the request of the Children of Israel in Surah Al-Baqara, and the request of the People of the Book in Surah An-Nisa. The objector argues that if vision were possible, the request would not be condemned. The commentator answers that the condemnation was not for the request itself but for the insolence and obstinacy with which it was made. This is supported by the fact that the same verses also condemn the request for angels to be sent down, which is not inherently impossible. Thus, the verses do not prove the impossibility of vision; they only condemn the attitude of the requesters.

Key Terms
الشبه السمعية
الاستعظام
التعنت
العناد
الرؤية
المعجزة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The Qur'anic verses prove that seeing God is absolutely impossible in any form.
Correction: The verses only condemn the requests made with obstinacy and insolence; they do not negate the possibility of a legitimate vision in the afterlife.

Checkpoint
What is the commentator's main response to the objection from the three verses that condemn asking to see God?
A) The verses only apply to this world
B) The condemnation was due to obstinacy and insolence, not the request itself
C) The verses are abrogated
D) The verses refer to vision in dreams

Key Takeaway
The Qur'anic condemnation of requests to see God was for the insolent attitude, not the request itself, so these verses do not contradict the possibility of beatific vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you make du'a, do you ever catch yourself demanding a sign from Allah as a condition for your faith, rather than submitting with humility? How does the condemnation of insolence in this lesson expose a potential deficiency in your own worship?
?
Think of a time you rejected someone's religious question as inappropriate or offensive. Was your rejection truly about the question itself, or was it about the attitude you perceived? How can you learn from the commentator's nuanced response to distinguish between genuine inquiry and insolence?
?
The Israelites were punished for their insolence in asking to see God. Is there an area of your life where you are more focused on demanding clarity or proof from Allah rather than trusting Him? What would it take for you to let go of that demand and walk in faith?

The 'Lan Tarani' Objection and Its Response
Al-Iji presents and refutes the third objection regarding the vision of God.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Al-Iji refutes the scriptural objection that the particle 'lan' in 'lan tarani' proves eternal prohibition of seeing God.

Core Idea
The particle 'lan' does not always mean 'never' — it can be limited to future negation.

This lesson examines a key scriptural objection against the vision of God: the verse 'lan tarani' (You will not see Me). The objectors claim that 'lan' indicates eternal negation, so if Moses cannot see God, no one can. Al-Iji responds by analyzing the linguistic scope of 'lan', showing it only negates the future, not all time. The commentary deepens this by comparing it to another verse.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its basis
Start by reading the objection: the verse 'lan tarani' and the claim that 'lan' indicates eternity. Understand the logical chain: if Moses never sees God, then by consensus no one else will.
Notice the assumption that 'lan' means 'never ever'.
2
Examine Al-Iji's linguistic answer
Read the response: Al-Iji argues that 'lan' is for emphatic negation in the future only, not for eternal abidance. He cites the verse about wishing for death (2:95) as evidence.
In 2:95, the disbelievers will not wish for death in this world but will wish for it in the Hereafter, proving 'lan' is limited.
3
Consider the commentary's nuance
The commentary notes that the analogy with 2:95 is imperfect because that verse's wording appears eternal but is interpreted as limited to worldly times. Reflect on the precision of Qur'anic language.
This shows the depth of scholarly debate.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
( الثالثة : ( من تلك الشبه (قوله تعالى لموسى : لن تراني ﴾ [الأعراف : ١٤٣ ] ، ولن للتأييد وإذا لم يره موسى ( أبدا ( لم يره غيره إجماعا . والجواب : منع كون لن للتأييد بل هو للنفي ( المؤكد ( في المستقبل فقط كقوله تعالى : ولن يتمنوه ﴾ [البقرة : ٩٥] أي الموت أبداً ، و لا شك أنهم ( يتمنوه في ( الآخرة ) للتخلص عن العقوبة قوله : ( لطلبهم الرؤية تعنتا و عناداً) وأيضاً جاز أن يكون الاستعظام لطلبهم الرؤية في الدنيا وعلى طريق الجهة والمقابلة على ما عرفوا من حال الأجسام والأعراض. قوله : ( كقوله تعالى : ولن يتمنوه أبدا ﴾ [البقرة : ٩٥])، لا يخفى أن نظم هذه الآية ظاهر في التأبيد ولما تحقق أنهم يتمنونه في الآخرة علم أن المراد التأبيد بالنسبة إلى أوقات الدنيا فمنع كون لن للتأبيد مطلقاً والتنظير بهذه الآية محل تأمل، اللهم إلا أن يقال : التأبيد الممنوع دلالة لن عليه هو التأبيد بالنسبة إلى جميع الأوقات، فتأمل .
The third [objection]: from those doubts: the statement of God Most High to Moses: 'You will not see Me' (Quran 7:143), and 'lan' is for eternity, and if Moses did not see Him ever, no one else will see Him by consensus. The answer: refuting that 'lan' is for eternity; rather it is for emphatic negation in the future only, like His statement: 'And they will never wish for it' (Quran 2:95), meaning death ever, and there is no doubt that they wish for it in the Hereafter to escape punishment. He said: (concerning 'their asking for vision obstinately and stubbornly') And also it is possible that the awe was for their asking for vision in the world and in the manner of direction and confrontation according to what they knew of the state of bodies and accidents. He said: (like His statement: 'And they will never wish for it ever' (Quran 2:95)): it is not hidden that the wording of this verse is apparent in eternity, and when it is verified that they wish for it in the Hereafter, it became known that what is intended is eternity with respect to worldly times; so refuting that 'lan' is for eternity absolutely, and analogizing with this verse is a place of reflection. Unless it is said: the eternity that 'lan' is prevented from indicating is eternity with respect to all times. So reflect.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This is the presentation of the third scriptural objection against the vision of God, followed by its answer and commentary. The objection quotes the verse 'lan tarani' (You will not see Me) and claims that the particle 'lan' indicates eternal negation, so if Moses was never allowed to see God, then by consensus no one else can either. The answer argues that 'lan' does not denote eternal abidance but simply emphatic negation with respect to the future, as seen in another verse where the same particle is used for the disbelievers' wish for death, yet they will wish for it in the Hereafter. The commentary then explains that the verse about wishing for death is not a perfect analogy because its wording apparently indicates eternity, but interpreted as eternity limited to this world. The commentator suggests that the true import of 'lan' regarding abidance is open to reflection.

التأييد
الرؤية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The particle 'lan' always indicates eternal negation in Arabic.
Correction: Al-Iji demonstrates that 'lan' only negates the future emphatically, as in Quran 2:95 where the negation is limited to worldly times.

Checkpoint
How does Al-Iji refute the argument that 'lan tarani' proves eternal prohibition of seeing God?
A) By claiming the verse is abrogated
B) By arguing that 'lan' only emphasizes future negation, not eternal abidance
C) By asserting that Moses did see God
D) By denying the authenticity of the verse

Key Takeaway
The particle 'lan' does not necessarily denote eternal negation; its scope must be understood from context and other Qur'anic usages.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?

?
The response uses a seemingly contradictory verse (Quran 2:95) to reinterpret 'lan'. Are you willing to adjust your understanding of a clear verse based on another verse's implication? What does that require of you in terms of humility before the Quran as a whole?
?
If 'lan' does not always mean eternal, what are you relying on to be certain of any theological claim derived from a single Quranic phrasing? How will you ensure your conviction is built on the full scope of revelation, not just a surface reading?

The Fourth Objection on God's Speech and Its Answer
Al-Iji presents the fourth objection using the verse on God's speech and answers it.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the fourth objection against the vision of God based on Quran 42:51, and the scholarly response that reconciles the verse with the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife.

Core Idea
The verse does not preclude vision; it only describes speech modes in this world.

The fourth objection attempts to prove that no one can ever see God by citing a Quranic verse that enumerates how God speaks to humans. If vision is not mentioned among these modes, the objectors argue, it cannot occur. The answer shows that the verse refers to this world, and that revelation can coincide with vision.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection and its premises
Carefully read the objection which cites Quran 42:51. Notice how it limits God's speech to three modes: revelation, from behind a veil, or via a messenger. The objection then draws a conclusion about vision.
The objectors claim that since none of these modes involve seeing God at the time of speech, and if God does not cause vision then, it will never happen.
2
Identify the answer's first part
The first answer clarifies that revelation (al-wahy) can occur during vision. Thus the verse does not preclude seeing God.
This makes revelation a possible context for vision.
3
Examine the interpretation about 'dar al-taklif'
The answer also suggests that the verse's restriction applies only to this worldly life (dar al-taklif), not the afterlife. Hence vision after death is not ruled out.
This reconciles the verse with other proofs affirming vision in Paradise.
4
Understand the discussion on 'from behind a veil'
The commentary analyzes the phrase 'from behind a veil' as meaning one sees something but does not hear speech directly, like Moses and the tree.
This shows that even in this mode, vision of the speaking entity is not negated.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الرابعة : ( منها ( قوله تعالى : وما كان لبشر أن يكلمه الله إلا وحياً أو من وراء حجاب أو يرسل رسولاً فيوحي بإذنه ما يشاء الشورى : ٥١] ، حصر تكليمه للبشر في الوحي إلى الرسل وتكليمه لهم من وراء حجاب وإرساله إياهم إلى الأمم ليكلمهم على ألسنتهم ( وإذا لم يرده من يكلمه في وقت الكلام لم يره في غيره إجماعاً ) وإذا لم يره هو أصلاً لم يره غيره أيضاً، إذ لا قائل بالفرق . ( والجواب : التكليم وحياً قد يكون حال الرؤية فإن الوحي كلام يسمع بسرعة قوله : ( أو يرسل رسولاً ليس هذا تكلماً في الحقيقة ولذا لم يورده المصنف فكأنه قال : حصر التكلم الحقيقي في اثنين. قوله : (لم يره في غيره إجماعاً ) قيل : حصر التكلم في الأمور المذكورة إنما هو في دار التكليف جمعاً بين الأدلة ونحن لا نقول بالرؤية فيها ويؤيده ما قيل في ونحن لا نقول بالرؤية فيها ويؤيده ما قيل في سبب نزول الآية أن اليهود قالوا لنبينا محمد عل الله ليس النبي إلا من كلمه الله تعالى وينظر إليه كما كلم موسى عليه السلام ونظر إليه فلا نؤمن بك حتى يحصل لك ذلك فقال له : لم ينظر موسى عليه السلام إلى الله فنزلت الآية لتصديقه فتأمل. قوله : ( والجواب أن التكليم وحياً قد يكون حال الرؤية بل ينبغي أن يحمل عليها ليصلح جعله قسيما لقوله : أو من وراء حجاب إذ لا معنى له سوى كونه بدون الرؤية كذا في تفسير القاضي وشرح المقاصد وفيه بحث لأن الحصر المذكور لا بد أو يخرج شيئا ولو حمل الوحي حالة الرؤية والحجاب على عدمها لم يبق حاله خارجة عنهما وقد يقال : معنى التكلم من وراء حجاب أن يرى شيئاً ولا يسمع منه كلام الله تعالى بلا كيف له فيه كشجرة موسى عليه السلام في الوادي الأيمن فيصلح قسيماً لإرسال الرسل وللوحي فإن الوحي يكون بإلقاء في القلب إما في اليقظة أو في النوم .
The fourth [objection]: from them: His statement Most High: 'And it is not for any human that God should speak to him except by revelation or from behind a veil or that He sends a messenger to reveal by His permission what He wills' (Quran 42:51). He restricted His speaking to humans: to revelation to messengers, and speaking to them from behind a veil, and sending them to nations to speak to them in their tongues. And if He does not intend to make him see at the time of speech, then by consensus He will not make him see at any other time. And if He does not make him see at all, He will not make anyone else see either, for there is no one who claims a difference. The answer: revelation may be at the moment of vision, for revelation is speech heard quickly. He said: (or sends a messenger) – this is not actually speech, so the author did not mention it, as if he said: restriction of real speech to two. He said: (he will not be made to see at any other time by consensus). It is said: the restriction of speech to the mentioned matters is only in the abode of obligation, reconciling the evidences, and we do not affirm vision therein. It is supported by what is said about the cause of revelation: the Jews said to our Prophet Muhammad—may God bless him—'A prophet is only one whom God speaks to and he looks at Him, as He spoke to Moses and he looked at Him, so we will not believe in you until you attain that.' So he said to him: 'Moses did not look at God,' and the verse was revealed to confirm him. So reflect. He said: (and the answer that revelation may be at the moment of vision, but it should be interpreted as such to make it a counterpart to His saying 'or from behind a veil', for that has no meaning other than being without vision) – as in the commentary of al-Qadi and Sharh al-Maqasid. There is a discussion because the mentioned restriction must exclude something; if revelation is taken as the state of vision and the veil as absence of vision, then no state remains outside them. And it may be said: the meaning of 'from behind a veil' is that one sees something but does not hear the speech of God Most High, without modality, like the tree of Moses in the right valley, so it becomes a counterpart to sending messengers and revelation, for revelation is by casting into the heart either in wakefulness or sleep.
Source pages p. 159

Commentary
This is the fourth scriptural objection against the vision of God and its answer, with commentary. The objection cites the Quranic verse restricting God's speech to humans to three modes: revelation, from behind a veil, or sending a messenger. The argument claims that if God does not cause the person spoken to to see Him at the time of speech, then by consensus that person will never see Him; consequently, since this restriction implies no vision occurs in any of these modes, no one can ever see God. The answer is that revelation can occur during vision, so the verse does not preclude seeing God. The commentary expands on this: the author (likely al-Iji) is said to limit real speech to two modes (revelation and from behind a veil), while sending a messenger is not real speech. Furthermore, the restriction is interpreted as applying only to the worldly life (dar al-taklif), not the afterlife. The commentary also discusses a report that the verse was revealed to deny the Jewish claim that Moses saw God, and it concludes by analyzing the meaning of 'from behind a veil' as seeing but not hearing.

Key Terms
تكليم
وحي
حجاب
رؤية
دار التكليف

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That Quran 42:51 exhaustively describes all possible modes of divine communication for all time, making vision of God impossible.
Correction: The verse is understood to refer only to this worldly life, and revelation can occur during vision, so it does not negate the possibility of seeing God in the afterlife.

Checkpoint
What is the key premise of the fourth objection regarding the vision of God?
A) God cannot speak to humans
B) The verse restricts God's speech to three modes, none of which involve vision during speech
C) Revelation is slower than vision
D) Only Moses could see God

Key Takeaway
The fourth objection fails because the verse's restriction applies to this world and revelation can include vision.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you read the objection that seeing God is impossible because the verse limits His speech, do you feel a sense of despair at perhaps never being able to behold your Creator? How does the answer—that this restriction is only for this world—transform your hope? What will you do today to increase your longing for that vision?
?
The Jews demanded to see God as a condition for belief. Are there any demands or conditions you have placed on your faith, expecting God to show you something before you trust Him? What does this lesson teach you about the nature of divine concealment in this world?
?
Reflect on the statement that revelation can occur during vision. Have you ever experienced a moment where divine guidance (like inspiration) came to you in a state of deep spiritual awareness? How can you create more such moments in your life, preparing for the ultimate vision in the hereafter?

The Price-Setter: Allah or Human? — A Debate in Kalam
Al-Amidi discusses the attribution of price-setting to Allah or human action, citing a hadith.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why the dispute over who sets prices is considered primarily a semantic issue in kalam, despite its roots in a prophetic hadith.

Core Idea
The dispute over price-setting is about language, not divine causation.

This passage from al-Amidi addresses a curious theological debate: is the price-setter (al-musa'ir) Allah or the human servant? The Ash'ari position relies on a hadith where the Prophet refused to set prices, saying 'Allah is the price-setter.' Yet the Mu'tazilites split on whether price-setting is a direct human act or generated by God's actions. The commentator ultimately notes that this is more a linguistic discussion than a real theological disagreement. Let's unpack the arguments.

Guided Path
1
Read the source passage
Read the Arabic passage and its translation carefully. Notice the hadith and the two opposing views.
2
Compare the Ash'ari and Mu'tazilite arguments
Identify how each side justifies its position. The Ash'ari use the hadith; the Mu'tazilites argue from human agreement or divine generation.
3
Reflect on the commentator's remark
Consider why the commentator says this is a linguistic dispute. How does this shift the nature of the debate?
4
Check your understanding
Answer the checkpoint quiz to confirm you grasped the key point.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الخامس في الأسعار) وهو الرخص والغلاء ( المسعر هو الله على أصلنا كما ورد في الحديث حين وقع غلاء في المدينة فاجتمع أهلها إليه عليه الصلاة والسلام وقالوا : سعر لنا يا رسول الله ، فقال : « المسعر الله» . ( وأما عندهم فمختلف فيه فقال بعضم : هو ( أي السعر ( فعل مباشر من العبد، إذ ليس ذلك إلا مواضعة منهم على البيع والشراء بثمن مخصوص، وقال آخرون : هو متولد من فعل الله تعالى ( وهو تقليل الأجناس وتكثير الرغبات بأسباب هي فعله تعالى . قوله : ( الخامس في الأسعار إلخ الظاهر ان هذا بحث لغوي لا كلامي إذ لا نزاع في أن تقليل الأجناس وتكثير الرغبات من الله تعالى، ولا في أن تعيين المبيع والثمن من العبد وإنما النزاع في إطلاق السعر لكن لما ورد في الحديث تعرضوا له
The fifth on prices (al-as'ār), which is cheapness and dearness. The price-setter (al-musa'ir) is God according to our principle, as is related in the hadith when a high price occurred in Medina, and the people gathered to him (peace be upon him) and said: 'Set a price for us, O Messenger of God.' He said: 'The price-setter is God.' As for according to them (the Mu'tazilites), there is disagreement: some said that price is a direct act of the servant, for it is nothing but an agreement among them on buying and selling at a specific price. Others said it is generated from the act of God Almighty, which is the reduction of species and increase of desires by means that are His acts. The commentator said: 'The fifth on prices...' apparently this is a linguistic discussion, not a theological one, since there is no dispute that the reduction of species and increase of desires are from God Almighty, nor that the determination of the commodity and price is from the servant; the only dispute is in the application of the term 'price' (sirr), but because it came in the hadith, they discussed it.
Source pages p. 191

Commentary
This passage discusses the theological attribution of prices (cheapness and dearness). The author states the Ash'ari position: the price-setter (al-musa'ir) is Allah, based on the hadith where the Prophet refused to set prices, saying 'Allah is the price-setter.' Then he presents the Mu'tazilite disagreement: some say price is directly the servant's act, since it is merely an agreement on a price; others say price is generated (mutawallid) from God's act, i.e., the reduction of supply and increase of demand are from God. The commentator remarks that this is primarily a linguistic dispute about the term 'price' (al-sirr) rather than a substantive theological disagreement, because both sides agree that God creates the underlying causes and humans set the specific price; they only differ on whether to call that 'setting the price'. The discussion is included because of the hadith.

Key Terms
الأسعار
المسعر
الرخص
الغلاء
المتولد

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: The issue is about whether God controls prices directly.
Correction: Rather, it is a semantic dispute about the application of the term 'price-setter'; both sides agree that God creates the underlying causes and humans set the specific price.

Checkpoint
What is the Ash'ari position on who sets prices?
A) Allah alone, based on the hadith.
B) The servants, by mutual agreement.
C) Both Allah and servants equally.
D) Angels appointed for that purpose.

Key Takeaway
The dispute over price-setting in kalam is fundamentally about the application of the term 'price-setter', not about the locus of causal power.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Have you ever argued with someone about a religious term, only to realize later that you both agreed on the underlying reality? Will you now pause before debating labels and first clarify what each of you means?
?
When the Prophet said 'al-musa'ir Allah', the commentator noted it was not a theological dispute. How often do you use prophetic statements to win arguments rather than to seek truth? What would change if you examined your intention before citing a hadith?
?
The Mu'tazilites disagreed among themselves about price-setting. In your own disagreements with fellow Muslims, do you first check whether the difference is about words or about substance? Commit today to asking, 'What do you mean by that?' before defending your position.

Dispute on Compensation for Animals' Pains
Enumeration of questions and disagreements about compensating animals for their pains.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the series of disputed questions about whether animals receive compensation for their pains and the motivations behind denying that animals feel pain.

Core Idea
The compensation of animals for their pains sparks a cascade of theological questions.

This passage from al-Iji's al-Mawaqif presents a series of interconnected questions that theologians have debated regarding the compensation of animals for the pains they suffer in this world. The questions progress logically from whether compensation occurs at all, to where and how it might take place, and finally to the cognitive capacity of animals in the afterlife. The passage also notes a position of outright denial of animal pain, revealing the theological stakes behind these debates.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the chain of conditional questions starting with 'هل تعوض'.
2
Identify the main question
The passage begins with the question: Are animals compensated for the pains and hardships of their lives? Try to identify what the alternative positions are.
The word 'أو' indicates a choice between two possibilities.
3
Trace the conditional chain
Follow the sequence of 'if' statements: if compensated, is it in this world or the next? If in the next, is it in Paradise or elsewhere? If in Paradise, is a mind created in them?
Each question builds on the previous one.
4
Understand the denial position
The passage ends by noting that some theologians outright deny that animals and children feel pain. Ask yourself: why would they take such a position?
The text says it is to avoid the necessity of animals entering Paradise and having minds.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
السابع : البهائم هل تعوض بما الآلام والمشاق مدة حياتها وتمتاز بها عن أمثالها التي لا تقاسي مثلها أو لا تعوض؟ وإن عوضت فهل ذلك التعويض في الدنيا أو في الآخرة؟ وإذا كان في الآخرة فهل هو في الجنة أو في غيرها ؟ وإن كان في الجنة فهل يخلق فيها عقل تعقل به أنه جزاء وأنه دائم غير منقطع ؟ هذه اختلافاتهم على أن منهم من أنكر لحق الألم البهائم والصبيان مكابرة وهرباً من إلزام دخولها الجنة وخلق العقل فيها.
The seventh: Do animals receive compensation for the pains and hardships during their lives, and are they distinguished thereby from their likes that do not suffer the like, or are they not compensated? And if they are compensated, is that compensation in this world or the next? And if it is in the next, is it in Paradise or elsewhere? And if it is in Paradise, is a mind created in them with which they understand that it is a recompense and that it is everlasting and unceasing? These are their disagreements, although among them are those who denied the occurrence of pain to animals and children out of obstinacy and to avoid the necessity of their entering Paradise and the creation of mind in them.
Source pages p. 221

Commentary
This passage is a list of disputed questions regarding whether animals (and by implication children) receive compensation for the pains and hardships they endure. The first question is whether they are compensated at all. If they are, then the next question is whether the compensation is in this world or the afterlife. If in the afterlife, is it in Paradise or somewhere else? If in Paradise, is a mind (ʿaql) created in them so that they can understand that it is a recompense and that it is eternal? The passage then notes that some theologians deny that animals and children actually feel pain, doing so out of obstinacy or to avoid being forced to admit that they enter Paradise and are given minds. This is a presentation of the different positions (ikhtilāfāt) without taking a side.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some readers might think the passage is arguing that animals are definitely compensated or that they are not compensated.
Correction: The passage only reports the disagreements; it does not advocate for a specific position.

Checkpoint

A) Because animals have no souls

Key Takeaway
The passage outlines a logical progression of questions about animal compensation, revealing the theological stakes, and notes that some deny animal pain to avoid admitting animal afterlife rewards.

The Mu'tazilite Argument on Reward Without Merit and Its Refutation
Parries Mu'tazilite objection by denying ugliness of gratuitous reward and restricting its applicability.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the Mu'tazilite claim that reward without prior merit is rationally ugly, and the Ash'ari response that denies the premise and restricts ugliness to contingent beings.

Core Idea
Reward without prior merit is not ugly for Allah; ugliness only applies to those subject to it.

This appendix presents a key exchange between the Mu'tazilites and the Ash'aris over the purpose of religious obligations. The Mu'tazilites argue that reward without prior merit is rationally ugly, using the sultan-and-scavenger analogy. The response challenges both the premise and its applicability to Allah.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing. Then read the translation. Note the flow: objection, answer with example, response.
This is a single continuous argument; observe how each part connects.
2
Identify the Mu'tazilite argument
Find where the Mu'tazilites answer the objection about the purpose of obligations. They claim the purpose is to expose the servant to reward, but then argue that reward (including honor) without merit is ugly. Examine the sultan-and-scavenger example.
The example distinguishes between giving wealth and giving honor.
3
Analyze the Ash'ari response
The response has two parts: first, it denies that bestowing reward initially is ugly; second, even if it were ugly, ugliness only applies to beings to whom ugliness can be attributed, not to Allah.
Consider why this response refutes the Mu'tazilite claim.
4
Reflect on theological implications
Think about what this means for Allah's actions: He is not bound by human notions of ugliness. Obligations are not necessary to create merit; Allah can give reward freely.
This upholds the Ash'ari principle of divine sovereignty.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تذنيب : إذا قيل لهم : أنتم قد أوجبتم الغرض في أفعاله تعالى فما الغرض من هذه التكاليف الشاقة التي لا نفع فيها لله لتعاليه عنه ولا للعبد لأنها مشقة بلا حظ ؟ قالوا : الغرض فيها عائد إلى العباد وهو ) تعريض العبد للثواب ( في الدار الآخرة وتمكينه منه ) فإن الثواب تعظيم) أي منفعة دائمة مقرونة بتعظيم وإكرام (وهو) أي التعظيم المذكور ( بدون استحقاق سابق قبيح عقلاً . ألا يرى أن السلطان إذا أمر بزبال وأعطاه من المال ما لا يدخل تحت الحصر لم يستقبح منه أصلا بل عد جودا وفضلا وإغناء للفقير وتبعيداً له عن ساحة الهوان بالكلية لكنه مع ذلك إذا نزل له وقام بين يديه معظماً له ومكرماً إياه وأمر خدمه بتقبيل أنامله استقبح منه ذلك وذمه العقلاء ونسبوه إلى ركاكة العقل وقلة الدراية فالله سبحانه لما أراد أن يعطي عباده منافع دائمة مقرونة بإجلال وإكرام منه ومن ملائكته المقربين ولم يحسن أن يتفضل بذلك عليهم ابتداء بلا استحقاق كلفهم ما يستحقونه .به فيقال لهم: لا نسلم أن التفضل بالثواب (قبيح بل لا قبح هناك أصلا ولو سلم قبحه فإنما يقبح ممن يجوز عليه
Appendix: If it is said to them: You have made purpose necessary in His actions, so what is the purpose of these burdensome obligations that contain no benefit for Allah—for He is exalted above it—nor for the servant, because they are hardship without advantage? They said: The purpose in them returns to the servants, and it is the exposure of the servant to reward in the hereafter and enabling him to attain it. For reward is a glorification, i.e., a permanent benefit accompanied by glorification and nobility. And is it, i.e., the aforementioned glorification, without prior merit ugly rationally? Do you not see that if a sultan commands a scavenger and gives him wealth beyond count, it is not considered ugly at all; rather it is considered generosity, favor, enrichment of the poor, and complete removal from the realm of humiliation. But if, in addition, he descends for him, stands before him, honors him, and orders his servants to kiss his fingers, that is considered ugly, and the intelligent condemn it and attribute it to weakness of mind and lack of discernment. So Allah, when He wanted to give His servants permanent benefits accompanied by veneration and honor from Him and His near angels, and it was not suitable that He bestow that upon them initially without merit, He obligated them with what they would merit. So it is said to them: We do not accept that bestowing reward initially is ugly; rather there is no ugliness there at all. And if its ugliness is granted, it is only ugly from one to whom ugliness applies.
Source pages p. 227

Commentary
This appendix (تذنيب) addresses a specific objection: if Allah must act for a purpose, what is the purpose of difficult religious obligations (تكاليف شاقة)? They bring no benefit to Allah, and they are hardship for the servant without immediate gain. The Mu'tazila (the 'them') answer that the purpose is to prepare the servant for reward (ثواب) in the afterlife, which is a permanent benefit with glorification. But this leads to a problem: giving such honor without prior merit is rationally ugly (قبيح عقلاً). They illustrate with a sultan giving wealth to a scavenger (permissible and generous) but then also bowing and honoring him (ugly). Therefore, Allah must create merit through obligations so that the reward becomes deserved. The response then rejects the premise: bestowing reward initially is not ugly. Even if it were, ugliness only applies to beings subject to ugliness, not to Allah. This refutes the Mu'tazilite claim and upholds that Allah can give reward without prior merit.

Key Terms
تذنيب
تكاليف شاقة
ثواب
تعظيم
استحقاق
قبيح عقلاً
تفضل

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Reward must be preceded by merit otherwise it is irrational or unjust.
Correction: The Ash'ari response shows that ugliness is not an objective category applying to Allah; He may give reward without merit, and the Mu'tazilite example fails because it analogizes human behavior to divine action.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the sultan-and-scavenger example in the Mu'tazilite argument?
A) That giving wealth without work is always ugly.
B) That honouring someone without prior merit is ugly.
C) That sultans must always act with purpose.
D) That obligations are unnecessary.

Key Takeaway
The Mu'tazilite argument that reward without prior merit is rationally ugly is refuted by denying the premise of ugliness and restricting moral categories to contingent beings.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you perform acts of worship, do you sometimes feel that Allah ‘owes’ you reward because of your effort? How does this passage challenge that assumption, and what would change if you truly believed that reward is a pure gift?
?
Have you ever judged Allah’s actions by what you consider ‘fair’ or ‘fitting’, like the Mu'tazilites did with the sultan example? Where in your life are you imposing human standards on the Creator, and are you ready to surrender that judgment and accept His absolute freedom?
?
Imagine you stand before Allah tomorrow and He grants you Paradise without any merit of your own. Would you feel entitled to it, or would you be overwhelmed by gratitude? How can you cultivate that sense of undeserving gratitude today in your prayer and charity?

The Sixth Mawqif: Sam'iyyat and Its Four Marāṣid
Introduction to the sixth mawqif on sam'iyyat and listing of its four marāṣid.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the scope of the sixth mawqif on sam'iyyāt and the four marāṣid it contains, including the status of the imamate.

Core Idea
Sam'iyyāt are matters known through revelation, subdivided into three original and one non-original topic.

This lesson introduces the sixth major section of the book, which deals with sam'iyyāt. The author defines what sam'iyyāt means and then outlines the four sub-topics, or marāṣid, that will follow. One of these, the imamate, is noted as not being among the original theological topics.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
First, read the Arabic passage and its literal translation to get an overall sense of the content. Notice the definition of sam'iyyāt and the listing of four marāṣid.
2
Understand the definition of sam'iyyāt
The passage defines sam'iyyāt as matters that either revelation depends on (like prophethood) or that depend on revelation (like the afterlife). Reflect on why these categories are distinct.
3
Identify the four marāṣid
From the passage, note that there are four marāṣid: three on the matters mentioned (prophethood, afterlife, causes of happiness/misery) and one on the imamate.
The passage explicitly states that the imamate is not among the original matters.
4
Consider the status of the imamate
Why might the imamate be excluded from the original topics? The passage says it is not from the original (aṣliyya). This is a methodological point about the scope of kalām.
5
Reflect on the lesson's importance
This passage sets the stage for the entire sixth mawqif. Understanding this categorization helps you navigate the upcoming detailed discussions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
الموقف السادس في السمعيات ) أي في الأمور التي يتوقف عليها السمع كالنبوة أو تتوقف هي على السمع كالمعاد وأسباب السعادة والشقاوة من الإيمان والطاعة والكفر والمعصية ) وفيه مراصد ) أربعة : ثلاثة منها في الأمور التي ذكرناها وواحد منها في الإمامة وليست من الأصلية كما مر وسيأتي أيضاً .
The sixth station on the sam'iyyat (matters known through revelation) – i.e., on matters on which revelation depends, like prophethood, or which themselves depend on revelation, like the afterlife and the causes of happiness and misery from faith, obedience, disbelief, and disobedience. And it contains four observatories: three of them on the matters we have mentioned, and one of them on the imamate, and it [the imamate] is not among the original [matters] as has passed and will also come.
Source pages p. 241

Commentary
This passage introduces the sixth mawqif of the book, which deals with sam'iyyāt. It begins by defining sam'iyyāt as matters that either revelation depends on (like prophethood, since revelation comes through prophets) or that depend on revelation themselves (like the afterlife and the criteria for happiness and misery, which are known only through revealed texts). The passage then states that this mawqif contains four marāṣid (observatories): three covering the matters just mentioned (prophethood, afterlife, and the causes of happiness/misery), and one on the imamate. However, it clarifies that the imamate is not among the original (aṣliyya) topics of kalām, a point previously made and to be revisited.

Key Terms
السمعيات
المراصد
الإمامة
الأصلية

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the imamate is a core theological topic equal to matters like prophethood and the afterlife.
Correction: The passage clarifies that the imamate is not among the original (aṣliyya) matters of kalām, indicating its secondary status.

Checkpoint
How many marāṣid does the sixth mawqif contain, and how many are on the original sam'iyyāt?
A) Four, three on sam'iyyāt
B) Three, all on sam'iyyāt
C) Four, four on sam'iyyāt
D) Three, two on sam'iyyāt

Key Takeaway
The sixth mawqif covers sam'iyyāt through four marāṣid, three on core revealed topics and one on the imamate, which is a later addition.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Are you building your understanding of the afterlife and the causes of happiness and misery solely on revelation, or do you rely on your own opinions? This lesson teaches that these are only known through sam'iyyat. Examine your heart: what is the primary source of your certainty about what happens after death? If it is not the revealed texts, what are you resting your hope on?
?
The passage distinguishes between original and non-original topics. The imamate is a later addition to kalām. Reflect: Are there issues in your own faith practice that you have elevated to the level of ‘original’ matters, while they are actually secondary? How does this affect your priorities and your unity with other Muslims?
?
Prophethood is a matter that revelation depends on. How deeply have you considered the necessity of prophethood in your own understanding of God's guidance? Do you truly submit to the fact that without prophets, you would have no certain knowledge of what pleases God? Or do you trust your own reasoning over the prophetic message?
