Resurrection of the Original Parts: Answering the Objection of Shared Body Parts
Al-Jurjani presents and answers an objection about resurrecting body parts eaten by another human.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the distinction between original body parts and transient food parts resolves the objection that bodily resurrection is impossible when one human's parts are consumed by another.

Core Idea
Only the permanent original parts are resurrected, not consumed nutrients.

The idea that resurrection involves the same physical body raises a difficult question: what if one human eats another? Would the same body parts belong to two people? Al-Jurjani presents this objection and answers it by distinguishing between the original, lifelong parts of a person and the transient matter from food. This distinction shows that only the original parts matter for resurrection, removing the contradiction.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Begin by reading the Arabic passage and its English translation. Notice the structure: the objection is stated briefly, then the answer is given.
The passage is short but dense. Read it slowly.
2
Identify the objection and the key distinction
Find where the objector's claim is presented. Then locate Al-Jurjani's answer, focusing on the phrase 'original parts' (الأجزاء الأصلية). Understand what makes them original versus transient.
The answer hinges on the difference between parts that remain throughout life and those that come and go with food.
3
Reflect on how the answer resolves the problem
Consider why the original parts of the eaten person are 'surplus' in the eater. This means they are not owed to the eater, so they can be returned to the eaten without duplication.
This resolves the apparent impossibility of resurrecting both individuals.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كما زعمتم . ( الجواب : إن المعاد إنما هو الأجزاء الأصلية وهي الباقية من أول العمر إلى آخره لا جميع الأجزاء ) على الإطلاق ) وهذه ) أي الأجزاء الأصلية التي كانت للإنسان المأكول ( في الأكل فضل فإنا نعلم أن الإنسان باق مدة عمره وأجزاء الغذاء تتوارد عليه وتزول عنه)، وإذا كانت فضلا فيه لم يجب إعادتها في الآكل بل في المأكول .
As you claimed. (The answer: The resurrection is only of the original parts, which remain from the beginning of life to its end, not all parts absolutely) and these (i.e., the original parts that were of the eaten human are surplus in the eater, for we know that a human remains throughout his life while food parts come and go), and if they are surplus in him, they must not be returned in the eater but rather in the eaten.
Source pages p. 323

Commentary
This passage is an objection-and-answer. The objector claims that if a human is eaten, his body parts become part of the eater, so resurrecting both would require the same parts in two bodies, which is impossible. The answer distinguishes between the original parts (الأجزاء الأصلية) that persist throughout life and the transient nutrients from food. The original parts of the eaten person remain in the eater's body as surplus – they pass through but are not incorporated as original parts. Therefore, they are not owed to the eater but to the eaten. Thus resurrection can proceed without contradiction.

Key Terms
الأجزاء الأصلية
المعاد
الآكل
المأكول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some people think that all physical parts that ever entered the body must be resurrected, leading to contradictions in cases of cannibalism.
Correction: The passage clarifies that only the original parts that remain from the beginning of life to the end are resurrected, not the transient food matter.

Checkpoint
According to the answer, what parts of the body are resurrected?
A) All parts that ever existed in the body
B) Only the original parts that remain throughout life
C) The parts present at death
D) No parts; only a spiritual resurrection

Key Takeaway
The resurrection concerns only the original, lifelong parts of a person, not the transient matter from food, thus resolving the objection of shared body parts.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you truly believe that Allah can restore you — every original part — even if your body has been completely destroyed and scattered? How does that certainty (or lack of it) affect your daily choices and fear of death?
?
The passage draws a sharp line between what is permanently 'yours' (original parts) and what is transient (nutrients). In your spiritual life, what have you been clinging to as permanent that is actually borrowed and passing — status, relationships, health? What would it mean to invest only in what endures?
?
The objector's confusion came from overthinking the physical process and limiting Allah's power. Have you ever let 'how could it be?' block you from accepting what Allah has decreed? Where in your life right now are you demanding to understand the mechanism before submitting to the truth?

Refutation of the Claim that Pleasure is Only Removal of Pain
Al-Jurjani refutes reducing pleasure to pain-removal, especially for the afterlife.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why equating pleasure with removal of pain is unproven, especially for afterlife pleasures, and why empirical induction cannot determine their nature.

Core Idea
Afterlife pleasures are a positive reality beyond worldly experience, not mere pain removal.

This passage from al-Jurjani's commentary challenges a common philosophical reduction: that pleasure is nothing but the removal of pain. Al-Jurjani grants this might be true for worldly pleasures but denies its proof and argues it cannot extend to the afterlife. He highlights the limits of empirical induction and defends the unique, positive nature of eternal pleasures.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the literal translation to grasp the flow of the argument.
Notice the concessional tone at the beginning: 'We concede...'
2
Identify the concessional strategy
The author first concedes for argument's sake that worldly bodily pleasure may be removal of pain, but he immediately denies that this is proven. Identify the two 'نسلم' statements.
3
Analyze the key inference denied
The objector infers that if worldly pleasure is removal of pain, then afterlife pleasure must be the same. Al-Jurjani rejects this by pointing out that afterlife pleasures could be different in reality despite similar form.
Focus on the phrase 'مشابهة للدنيوية صورة ومخالفة لها حقيقة.'
4
Understand the epistemic limitation
Al-Jurjani argues that empirical experience (وجدان) and induction (استقراء) only apply to worldly matters, not to the afterlife. This blocks the objector's argument.
The phrase 'ولا مجال للوجدان والاستقراء فيها' is central.
5
Reflect on the theological implication
Consider why this refutation matters: it defends the unique, positive reality of afterlife rewards, preserving their distinctness from worldly pleasures.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نسلم أن اللذة ) الجسمانية لا حقيقة لها وأنها ) دفع الألم غايته أن في دفع الألم لذة وإما أنها ليست إلا هو ( أي دفع الألم ( فلا دليل عليه ( ولم لا يجوز أن تكون ) تلك اللذة ( أمراً آخر يحصل معه)، أي مع دفع الألم (تارة ودونه أخرى) والدوران وجوداً وعدماً في بعض الصور لا ينافي ما ذكرناه (سلمنا ذلك في اللذات الدنيوية فلم قلتم أن اللذات ) الجسمانية ( الأخروية كذلك ) أي دفع الألم ( ولم لا يجوز أن تكون اللذات الأخروية مشابهة للدنيوية صورة ومخالفة لها حقيقة، فتكون حقيقة هذه ) الدنيوية (دفع الألم) كما ادعيتم ( وحقيقة تلك) الأخروية (أمراً آخر) وجودياً، ولا مجال للوجدان والاستقراء فيها أي في اللذات الأخروية حتى يدرك بهما حقيقتها كما أدركت حقيقة الدنيوية بهما على زعمكم
We concede that bodily pleasure has no reality and that it is the removal of pain—at best, there is pleasure in removal of pain—but that it is nothing but removal of pain, there is no proof for that. And why may it not be that pleasure is something else that occurs sometimes with removal of pain and sometimes without? Concomitance in existence and non-existence in some cases does not negate what we mentioned. We concede that concerning worldly pleasures, but why do you say that otherworldly bodily pleasures are likewise (i.e., removal of pain)? And why may it not be that otherworldly pleasures resemble worldly ones in form but differ in reality, so that the reality of worldly pleasures is removal of pain as you claim, while the reality of otherworldly pleasures is something else, positive? And there is no room for empirical experience and induction concerning otherworldly pleasures to perceive their reality as you claim to have perceived the reality of worldly pleasures through them.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of the claim that pleasure is merely the removal of pain. The author first concedes for argument's sake that worldly bodily pleasure may be only the removal of pain, but he denies that this is proven. He then argues that even if it were true for worldly pleasures, it does not follow for afterlife pleasures. Otherworldly pleasures could resemble worldly ones in form but be entirely different in reality—a positive existence, not just removal. Moreover, we cannot rely on empirical experience or induction for the afterlife, so the objector's argument is not conclusive.

Is Annihilation Before Resurrection Proven?
Al-Jurjani denies that annihilation before resurrection is proven.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why al-Jurjani concludes that neither annihilation nor mere scattering is conclusively proven from scripture.

Core Idea
Resurrection's mechanism is not scripturally fixed—only the fact is certain.

In this short appendix, al-Jurjani addresses a theological question: does God annihilate the body's parts before recreating them, or does He scatter and recompose? He concludes that neither view is established by proof, and the Quranic verses often cited are insufficient. This lesson explores his reasoning and the limits of scriptural evidence on such details.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Read the Arabic passage once without analyzing. Then read the translation. Let the author's own argument come through.
Notice the technical terms like الإعدام and التفريق.
2
Identify the author's conclusion
The author states that the truth (الحق) is that neither annihilation nor scattering is proven. No certainty in denial or affirmation.
This is a position of suspending judgment (tawaqquf).
3
Examine the scriptural evidence
The author examines two verses: Qasas 88 and Rahman 26. He argues they do not prove annihilation because 'perishing' (هلاك) includes scattering and loss of composition.
Understand why the evidence is called 'weak in indication' (ضعيف الدلالة).

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تذنيب . هل يعدم الله الأجزاء البدنية ثم يعيدها أو يفرقها ويعيد فيها التأليف ؟ الحق أنه لم يثبت ذلك ولا جزم فيه نفياً ولا إثباتاً لعدم الدليل على شيء من الطرفين، ( وما يحتج به ) على الإعدام ) من قوله تعالى : كلُّ شيء هالك إلا وجهه ﴾ [ القصص : ۸۸] ضعيف) الدلالة عليه، لأن التفريق (هلاك كالإعدام ) فإن هلاك كل شيء خروجه عن صفاته المطلوبة منه وزوال التأليف الذي به تصلح الأجزاء لأفعالها وتتم منافعها والتفريق بالرفع عطفاً على زوال يجري منه مجرى التفسير وقوله ( كذلك ) خبر لهما أي زوال التأليف والتفريق خروج للشيء عن صفاته المطلوبة منه فيكون هلاكاً ومثله يسمى فناء عرفاً فلا يتم الاستدلال بقوله تعالى : كل من عليها فان ﴾ [الرحمن: ٢٦] ، على الإعدام أيضاً
Appendix. Does God annihilate the bodily parts then recreate them, or does He scatter them and restore composition? The truth is that this has not been established, nor is there certainty in denying or affirming it, due to the lack of proof on either side. And what is used as evidence for annihilation from His statement 'Everything is perishable except His Face' [Qasas 88] is weak in indication, because scattering is a destruction like annihilation, for the destruction of a thing is its departure from its desired attributes and the removal of the composition by which parts are fit for their actions and their benefits are completed; and scattering, through raf' (as a conjunct to 'removal'), runs as an interpretation of it. And His statement 'likewise' is a predicate for both, i.e., removal of composition and scattering are a departure of the thing from its desired attributes, so it is destruction, and the like is called 'annihilation' conventionally. Thus the inference from His statement 'Everyone upon it is perishing' [Rahman 26] for annihilation is also not established.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a brief discussion (تذنيب) on whether God annihilates bodily parts before resurrection or merely scatters and re-composes them. The author states that neither position is proven. The evidence for annihilation from the Quranic verse 'Everything is perishable except His Face' (Qasas 88) is weak, because 'perishable' (هالك) includes scattering as a form of destruction: a thing's destruction means loss of its intended attributes and composition. The verse 'Everyone upon it is perishing' (Rahman 26) is also not conclusive for annihilation in the sense of non-existence.

Key Terms
الإعدام
التفريق
التأليف
الهلاك
الفناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some assume that resurrection requires complete annihilation of body parts before recreation.
Correction: Al-Jurjani shows that neither annihilation nor scattering is proven, and the Quranic verses are not conclusive for annihilation.

Checkpoint
What does al-Jurjani conclude about whether God annihilates body parts before resurrection?
A) He affirms annihilation
B) He denies annihilation
C) He says it is not proven either way
D) He says it is obligatory to believe in annihilation

Key Takeaway
The mechanism of resurrection—whether by annihilation or mere scattering—remains unproven in classical Islamic theology, as scriptural evidence is ambiguous.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you have a fixed mental image of how resurrection will occur that is not explicitly taught in the Qur’an or Sunnah? Are you willing to leave that image unconfirmed and trust God’s wisdom, as al-Jurjani does when he says 'the truth is that this has not been established'?
?
When you read verses like 'Everything is perishable except His Face', do you automatically assume annihilation, or do you pause to consider other possibilities? What does your immediate assumption reveal about how much you rely on your own reasoning versus revealed texts?
?
Al-Jurjani refrains from affirming either annihilation or scattering due to lack of proof. Is there an area of your faith where you have insisted on a specific mechanism or detail that God has left ambiguous? How can you cultivate the humility to say 'I do not know' and still trust God's power and wisdom?

Three of Five Views on Resurrection
Al-Jurjani enumerates three of five possible views on resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the first three possible positions on resurrection as classified by Al-Jurjani and who advocates each.

Core Idea
Only five possible views exist on the nature of resurrection.

Al-Jurjani begins his discussion of eschatological matters by mapping the logical possibilities. He asserts that there are exactly five possible positions regarding resurrection, and then lists the first three. This passage identifies the main factions in Islamic theology and philosophy, and their stances on whether the resurrection is bodily, spiritual, or both.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once through, then read the translation. Notice the enumeration: the author states there are five views, but only three are given in this excerpt.
The passage is cut off; the remaining two views likely involve combinations or modalities.
2
Identify the three views and their proponents
Match each view (first, second, third) to the group that holds it. What is the defining characteristic of each group? For example, why do 'most theologians who deny the rational soul' hold the first view?
3
Reflect on the logical completeness
Ask yourself why the author insists that the possible positions are exactly five. What logical principle might underlie this enumeration? Consider that each position relates to the combination of body and soul in the afterlife.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
واعلم أن الأقوال الممكنة في مسألة المعاد لا تزيد على خمسة. الأول : ثبوت المعاد الجسماني فقط وهو قول أكثر المتكلمين النافين للنفس الناطقة، والثاني : ثبوت المعاد الروحاني فقط وهو قول الفلاسفة الإلهيين، والثالث : ثبوتهما معاً وهو قول كثير من المحققين كالحليمي والغزالي والراغب وأبي زيد الدبوسي ومعمر من قدماء المعتزلة وجمهور من متأخري الإمامية وكثير من الصوفية
And know that the possible positions on the question of resurrection do not exceed five. The first: affirmation of bodily resurrection only – this is the view of most theologians who deny the rational soul. The second: affirmation of spiritual resurrection only – this is the view of the divine philosophers. The third: affirmation of both together – this is the view of many verifiers such as al-Halimi, al-Ghazali, al-Raghib, Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, Muammar from the early Mu'tazila, the majority of later Imamiyya, and many Sufis.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a classification of the possible views on resurrection. The author states there are only five possible positions. He lists the first three: (1) bodily resurrection only, held by theologians (mutakallimun) who deny an immaterial soul; (2) spiritual resurrection only, held by the divine philosophers; (3) both bodily and spiritual resurrection, held by many leading scholars like al-Halimi, al-Ghazali, al-Raghib, Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, Muammar from early Mu'tazila, most later Imamiyya, and many Sufis. The text breaks off, so the remaining two views are not included in this passage, but they would likely be about the nature of the body or soul in resurrection.

Key Terms
المعاد الجسماني
المعاد الروحاني
النفس الناطقة
الفلاسفة الإلهيين

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception: The only two views on resurrection are either purely bodily or purely spiritual.
Correction: Al-Jurjani lists five possible positions, including a third view that combines both bodily and spiritual resurrection, showing intermediate possibilities.

Checkpoint

Key Takeaway

?

Refuting the Claim that Bodily Pleasure is Only Removal of Pain
The author refutes the claim that all bodily pleasure is nothing but removal of pain.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how the author refutes the objection that all bodily pleasure is simply the removal of pain, and why otherworldly pleasures cannot be judged by worldly standards.

Core Idea
Pleasure is not identical to pain removal; otherworldly pleasures may differ in essence.

This passage presents a careful refutation of a common objection against the reality of bodily pleasures in the afterlife. The opponent argues that bodily pleasure is nothing but the removal of pain, thus denying its existential reality. The author concedes a partial point but skillfully distinguishes between accompaniment and identity, and between worldly and otherworldly realms.

Guided Path
1
Read the Arabic and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysing, then read the translation. Let the author's argument flow naturally.
2
Note the initial concession and distinction
Observe how the author concedes that pleasure may accompany removal of pain, but immediately denies that they are identical. This is a key move.
The Arabic phrase 'غايته أن في دفع الألم لذة' indicates the concession.
3
Identify the two main arguments
First, the possibility that pleasure is a separate thing that can occur with or without pain removal. Second, the distinction between worldly and otherworldly pleasures, and the limitation of empirical perception.
Look for 'ولم لا يجوز' and 'سلمنا ذلك في اللذات الدنيوية'.
4
Reflect on empirical perception
Consider why the author says empirical perception (الوجدان والاستقراء) cannot be applied to otherworldly pleasures. This highlights a methodological limit.
The phrase 'ولا مجال للوجدان والاستقراء فيها' is crucial.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
نسلم أن اللذة الجسمانية لا حقيقة لها وأنها دفع الألم غايته أن في دفع الألم لذة وإما أنها ليست إلا هو أي دفع الألم فلا دليل عليه ولم لا يجوز أن تكون تلك اللذة أمراً آخر يحصل معه أي مع دفع الألم تارة ودونه أخرى والدوران وجوداً وعدماً في بعض الصور لا ينافي ما ذكرناه سلمنا ذلك في اللذات الدنيوية فلم قلتم أن اللذات الجسمانية الأخروية كذلك أي دفع الألم ولم لا يجوز أن تكون اللذات الأخروية مشابهة للدنيوية صورة ومخالفة لها حقيقة، فتكون حقيقة هذه الدنيوية دفع الألم كما ادعيتم وحقيقة تلك الأخروية أمراً آخر وجودياً، ولا مجال للوجدان والاستقراء فيها أي في اللذات الأخروية حتى يدرك بهما حقيقتها كما أدركت حقيقة الدنيوية بهما على زعمكم
We concede that bodily pleasure has no reality and that it is removal of pain, at most that there is pleasure in removal of pain, but that it is nothing other than that (i.e., removal of pain), there is no proof for it. And why is it not possible that that pleasure is another thing that occurs sometimes with removal of pain and sometimes without it? And the conjunction in existence and non-existence in some cases does not contradict what we have mentioned. We concede this regarding worldly pleasures, so why do you say that otherworldly bodily pleasures are likewise (i.e., removal of pain)? And why is it not possible that otherworldly pleasures resemble worldly ones in form but differ from them in reality? So that the reality of these (worldly pleasures) is removal of pain, as you claim, and the reality of those (otherworldly pleasures) is another existential thing; and there is no scope for empirical perception and induction concerning them (i.e., otherworldly pleasures) to perceive their reality, as the reality of worldly pleasures was perceived by them according to your claim.
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a refutation of an objection. The opponent argues that bodily pleasure is nothing more than the removal of pain. The author first concedes that pleasure may accompany the removal of pain, but denies that it is identical to it. He points out that the fact that pleasure sometimes occurs with removal of pain and sometimes without it does not entail that it is the same thing. Moreover, even if worldly pleasures were merely removal of pain, one cannot extrapolate this to otherworldly pleasures because they may share the form but differ in essence. The objection is that otherworldly pleasures cannot be known through empirical observation or induction, so the opponent's claim is unfounded.

Key Terms

دفع الألم
الوجدان
الاستقراء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That bodily pleasure is necessarily identical to the removal of pain simply because it often accompanies it.
Correction: The author argues that accompaniment does not imply identity, and that otherworldly pleasures may be different in essence.

Checkpoint
What is the main point of the author's response to the objection about bodily pleasure?
A) He denies that bodily pleasure exists.
B) He concedes that all pleasure is removal of pain.
C) He argues that otherworldly pleasures may be different in reality from worldly ones.
D) He says empirical perception can be used for otherworldly pleasures.

Key Takeaway
The author refutes the objection by showing that pleasure is not identical to pain removal and that otherworldly pleasures cannot be judged by worldly empirical standards.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
You often feel pleasure only when discomfort ends—like relief from hunger or pain. But this passage suggests that true pleasure might be something more. Are you settling for a life that is merely the absence of pain, when Allah’s promise in Jannah offers something far greater? What would it mean for your strivings if you began to desire that higher pleasure?
?
The author argues that our limited experience cannot judge the reality of otherworldly pleasures. You trust your senses every day, but this lesson confronts you: are you willing to submit to revelation about realities you cannot test? Is your certainty about the unseen built on more than wishful thinking? How will you actively strengthen your conviction about that which is beyond your empirical grasp?
?
This refutation shows that the same form can conceal a different essence. Think of an act of worship: the outward form—prayer, fasting—may look the same, but the essence of your sincerity transforms it. Are you content with the form, or do you seek the essence? This lesson forces you to ask: when you perform a deed for Allah, is the pleasure you feel merely the removal of the discomfort of obligation, or something real and profound?

Annihilation vs. Dispersion of Body Parts
Author examines annihilation vs. dispersal and critiques Quranic proofs.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the author's argument that neither annihilation nor dispersion of body parts is definitively proven, and why Quranic verses on perishing do not settle the matter.

Core Idea
Dispersion is a form of destruction; no evidence proves either view.

Before examining the possible views on resurrection, the author addresses a preliminary question: does God annihilate the body parts or merely disperse them? He argues that there is no decisive evidence for either position, and that even Quranic verses about perishing do not provide clear proof, since dispersion itself constitutes destruction. This lesson explores that argument and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, noting the author's cautious tone. He does not assert a preference but states the lack of evidence.
Pay attention to the terms إعدام (annihilation) and تفريق (dispersion).
2
Understand the two positions
The author presents two options: God annihilates the parts and recreates them, or He disperses them and then reassembles. Both are considered possible but unproven.
3
Examine the critique of the Quranic proof
The author discusses the verse 'Everything is perishing except His Face' and explains why it does not prove annihilation. He defines destruction as loss of desired attributes and composition.
He also mentions that dispersion counts as destruction in common usage.
4
Reflect on the takeaway
Consider why the author leaves the matter undecided. This sets the stage for the five positions on resurrection.
The lack of certainty here is a methodological point about relying on clear evidence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
تذنيب . هل يعدم الله الأجزاء البدنية ثم يعيدها أو يفرقها ويعيد فيها التأليف ؟ الحق أنه لم يثبت ذلك ولا جزم فيه نفياً ولا إثباتاً لعدم الدليل على شيء من الطرفين، ( وما يحتج به ) على الإعدام ) من قوله تعالى : كلُّ شيء هالك إلا وجهه ﴾ [ القصص : ۸۸] ضعيف) الدلالة عليه، لأن التفريق (هلاك كالإعدام ) فإن هلاك كل شيء خروجه عن صفاته المطلوبة منه وزوال التأليف الذي به تصلح الأجزاء لأفعالها وتتم منافعها والتفريق بالرفع عطفاً على زوال يجري منه مجرى التفسير وقوله ( كذلك ) خبر لهما أي زوال التأليف والتفريق خروج للشيء عن صفاته المطلوبة منه فيكون هلاكاً ومثله يسمى فناء عرفاً فلا يتم الاستدلال بقوله تعالى : كل من عليها فان ﴾ [الرحمن: ٢٦] ، على الإعدام أيضاً واعلم أن الأقوال الممكنة في مسألة المعاد لا تزيد على خمسة. الأول : ثبوت المعاد الجسماني فقط وهو قول أكثر المتكلمين النافين للنفس الناطقة، والثاني : ثبوت المعاد الروحاني فقط وهو قول الفلاسفة الإلهيين، والثالث : ثبوتهما معاً وهو قول كثير من المحققين كالحليمي والغزالي والراغب وأبي زيد الدبوسي ومعمر من قدماء المعتزلة وجمهور من متأخري الإمامية وكثير من الصوفية في قوله : (النافين للنفس الناطقة والقائلين بأن النفس جسم إما هذا الهيكل المخصوص أو جسم داخل فيه وهو الأجزاء الأصلية كما علم من مذهبهم . قوله: (والثالث ثبوتهما معاً) قال في شرح الصحائف وهذا على وجهين: أحدهما أن يكون الروح مجرداً عن المادة فيعاد الجسم ويتعلق به الروح أو يتعلق بجسم آخر من غير إعادة الجسم الأول، وهذا مذهب قليل من أهل العلم كالغزالي والفارابي ومن تابعهما الثاني أن يكون الروح جسماً سماوياً ويعاد الجسم الأول ويورد فيه الروح وهو قول كثير من المسلمين وأكثر النصارى إلى هاهنا كلامه، فتأمل. والرابع : عدم ثبوت شيء منهما وهذا قول القدماء الطبعيين والخامس : التوقف في هذه الأقسام وهو المنقول عن جالينوس فإنه قال : لم يتبين لي أن النفس هل هي المزاج فينعدم عند الموت فيستحيل إعادتها، أو هي جوهر باق بعد فساد البنية فيمكن المعاد حينئذ
Appendix. Does God annihilate the bodily parts and then recreate them, or does He disperse them and restore their composition? The truth is that this is not established, nor is there a definitive judgment either way due to lack of evidence on either side. What is adduced as proof for annihilation from His saying, 'Everything is perishing except His Face' (Qasas 88) is weak in indication, because dispersion is destruction like annihilation. For the destruction of everything is its departure from its desired attributes and the removal of the composition through which the parts are fit for their actions and their benefits are completed; dispersion, by being in the accusative as a conjunction to 'removal', runs as an interpretation. And His saying 'likewise' is the predicate for both, i.e., removal of composition and dispersion are a departure of the thing from its desired attributes, so it is destruction, and the like is called annihilation conventionally. So the proof from His saying, 'Everyone upon it will pass away' (Rahman 26) for annihilation is also not fully established. Know that the possible positions on the matter of resurrection do not exceed five. First: affirmation of bodily resurrection only, which is the position of most theologians who deny the rational soul. Second: affirmation of spiritual resurrection only, which is the position of divine philosophers. Third: affirmation of both together, which is the position of many investigators such as al-Halimi, al-Ghazali, al-Raghib, Abu Zayd al-Dabusi, and Muammar from the early Mu'tazila, the majority of later Imamis, and many Sufis. In his saying: 'Deniers of the rational soul and those who say the soul is a body either this particular structure or a body inside it, which are the original parts, as known from their doctrine.' His saying: 'And the third is affirmation of both together' – he said in Sharh al-Sahayif: 'This is on two aspects: one is that the spirit is abstracted from matter, then the body is recreated and the spirit attaches to it, or it attaches to another body without recreating the first body; this is the position of a few scholars like al-Ghazali and al-Farabi and those who followed them. The second is that the spirit is a celestial body, and the first body is recreated and the spirit is placed in it; this is the position of many Muslims and most Christians.' Up to here is his speech. Reflect. Fourth: affirmation of neither, which is the position of ancient naturalists. Fifth: suspension of judgment regarding these categories, which is transmitted from Galen; for he said: 'It is not clear to me whether the soul is a temperament that perishes at death, making its return impossible, or it is a substance that remains after the corruption of the physical structure, in which case resurrection becomes possible.'
Source pages p. 324

Commentary
This passage is a detailed appendix (tadhneeb) examining whether God annihilates the body parts before resurrection or merely disperses them and then reassembles. The author first states that neither view is proven for lack of evidence. He critiques the proof from Quranic verses that everything perishes (Qasas 88 and Rahman 26), arguing that dispersion counts as annihilation in the sense of losing desired qualities. Then he enumerates five possible positions on resurrection: 1) bodily resurrection only (most mutakallimun who deny an immaterial soul); 2) spiritual resurrection only (divine philosophers); 3) both together (many investigators such as al-Halimi, al-Ghazali, al-Raghib, etc.) with two sub-views explained in the gloss; 4) neither (ancient naturalists); 5) suspension of judgment (Galen). The two glosses clarify the first and third positions.

Key Terms
إعدام
تفريق
هلاك
فناء

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: That annihilation (إعدام) and dispersion (تفريق) are essentially different and can be distinguished by the Quranic verses.
Correction: The author argues that dispersion is a form of destruction because it removes composition and desired attributes, so the Quranic verses about perishing do not settle the matter.

Checkpoint
What is the author's conclusion about whether God annihilates or disperses body parts?
A) He asserts that God annihilates them.
B) He asserts that God disperses them.
C) He says neither view is established due to lack of evidence.
D) He says both are proven.

Key Takeaway
Neither annihilation nor dispersion of body parts is proven; dispersion is a form of destruction, so Quranic verses on perishing do not decide the matter.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
When you reflect on your own body and its eventual fate, do you find it difficult to accept that some details—like whether God annihilates or disperses—may simply be unknown? How does this uncertainty affect your trust in God's plan for resurrection?
?
The author withholds judgment on this matter due to insufficient evidence. Are there doctrines about life after death that you have held confidently without examining the evidence? Will you now look more carefully at what the Quran and reason actually say?
?
You recite 'Everyone upon it will pass away' (Rahman 26) regularly. Does this lesson change how you understand that verse? Will you now pause to consider the deeper theological issues behind such familiar words?

Five Possible Positions on Resurrection
Author enumerates five possible positions on resurrection with sub-views.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to list and describe the five possible positions on resurrection as enumerated by the author, including the two sub-views of the third position.

Core Idea
Only five logically possible views on resurrection exist.

Having set aside the question of annihilation versus dispersion, the author now presents the full range of possible positions on resurrection. He enumerates five views, covering bodily, spiritual, combined, denial, and agnostic stances. The third position—affirming both body and spirit—is further divided into two sub-views. This lesson maps out these options and the major thinkers associated with each.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and identify the five positions
Read the Arabic and translation. As you read, note the five positions: 1) bodily only, 2) spiritual only, 3) both, 4) neither, 5) suspension.
Pay attention to the groups mentioned for each position.
2
Understand the third position in detail
The passage includes a gloss explaining two sub-views within the third position: one where the spirit is abstract and attaches to a body (recreated or new), and another where the spirit is a celestial body placed into the recreated body.
The gloss quotes Sharh al-Sahayif.
3
Reflect on the diversity of views
Consider how the author includes both classical and contemporary thinkers, from theologians to philosophers to Sufis, showing the breadth of Islamic thought on resurrection.

Proof that the Rational Soul Does Not Perish
Philosophers prove that the rational soul is simple and cannot be annihilated.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' argument that the rational soul, being simple and existing in actuality, cannot admit annihilation and therefore remains after death.

Core Idea
A simple, actually existing thing cannot be receptive to its own annihilation.

The philosophers of Islam argued for the immortality of the rational soul using a crisp logical proof. They claimed that because the soul is simple (has no parts) and already exists in actuality, it cannot simultaneously possess the potentiality for its own destruction. This lesson unpacks that argument, including a response to a possible objection.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
First, read the Arabic passage and its English translation to get the overall flow of the argument.
The passage begins with the philosophers' claim and then provides the proof.
2
Identify the key claim
Notice that the philosophers assert: 'The rational soul does not accept annihilation.' This is the conclusion they aim to prove.
3
Understand the proof structure
The proof is that the soul is simple (بسيطة) and exists in actuality. If it accepted annihilation, it would have both actuality (for existence) and potentiality (for annihilation), which is contradictory in a simple entity.
Contradictory attributes would require two distinct loci, violating simplicity.
4
Follow the objection and response
A hypothetical objection suggests that composition might allow the coexistence, but the philosophers respond by distinguishing the potentiality for existence (which belongs to bodily matter) from the case at hand.
The soul itself has no material substrate, so the objection fails.
5
Confirm the conclusion
The passage ends by reaffirming that since the soul does not accept annihilation, it remains after separation from the body.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
المقصد الثالث : في إنكار حشر الأجساد ( في حكاية مذهب الحكماء المنكرين لحشر الأجساد في أمر المعاد ) الروحاني الذي هو عندهم عبارة عن مفارقة النفس عن بدنها واتصالها بالعالم العقلي الذي هو عالم المجردات وسعادتها وشقاوتها هناك بفضائلها النفسانية ورذائلها ( قالوا : النفس الناطقة لا تقبل الفناء ؛ أي العدم بعد وجودها وذلك ) لأنها بسيطة ) لما مر مباحث النفس، وهي موجودة بالفعل فلو قبلت الفناء لكان للبسيط) الذي هو النفس حال كونها موجودة (فعل) بالنسبة إلى وجودها وقوة ) أي قابلة بالنسبة إلى فنائها وفسادها ( وإنه محال لأن حصول أمرين متنافيين لا يكون إلا في محلين متغايرين وهو ينافي البساطة)، وتلخيصه أن الموجود بالفعل لا يكون هو بعينه متصفاً بقابلية فنائه وفساده لأن القائل يجب بقاؤه مع حصول المقبول ولا بقاء لذلك الموجود مع الفناء والفساد فبين وجود شيء بالفعل وقابلية فنائه منافاة فلا يجتمعان في بسيط، فلو اجتمعا بمثل ما ذكرتموه ولم يلزم من ذلك تركبها قلنا : لأن المنصف بقابلية وجودها هو المادة البدنية الحاصلة عند حدوثها فلا حاجة إلى إثبات مادة لجوهر النفس بخلاف ما نحن فيه وإذا لم تقبل الناطقة الفناء كانت باقية بعد المفارقة
The Third Observational Post: On Denial of the Resurrection of Bodies. (Concerning the narration of the doctrine of the philosophers who deny the resurrection of bodies regarding the matter of resurrection) the spiritual resurrection, which according to them is the separation of the soul from its body and its connection to the intellectual world, which is the world of immaterial beings, and its happiness and misery there due to its psychic virtues and vices. They said: The rational soul does not accept annihilation, i.e., non-existence after its existence. And that is because it is simple, as has passed in the inquiries on the soul, and it exists in actuality. So if it accepted annihilation, then the simple (i.e., the soul) while existing in actuality would have both actuality with respect to its existence and potentiality (i.e., receptivity) with respect to its annihilation and corruption. And that is impossible because the occurrence of two contradictory things can only occur in two distinct loci, which contradicts simplicity. The summary is that a thing existing in actuality cannot itself be qualified by the potentiality for its own annihilation and corruption, because the recipient must continue to exist while the received occurs, and there is no continuation of that existent with annihilation and corruption. So between a thing's existence in actuality and its potentiality for annihilation there is incompatibility, so they cannot coexist in something simple. If they were to coexist as you mentioned and composition does not necessarily follow, we say: Because the locus of the potentiality for its existence is the bodily matter that occurs at its origination, so there is no need to posit a matter for the substance of the soul, unlike the case at hand. And if the rational soul does not accept annihilation, it remains after separation.
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage presents the philosophers' proof that the rational soul is immortal. The argument proceeds as follows: The rational soul is simple (بسيطة) and exists in actuality. If it could undergo annihilation, it would simultaneously possess actuality (for its existence) and potentiality (for its annihilation). But these two are contradictory because the same simple entity cannot have two opposing attributes that require distinct loci. The summary sharpens the point: an actually existing thing cannot at the same time be receptive to its own non-existence, because the recipient must survive the reception, but in annihilation the thing does not survive. Therefore, the soul cannot be subject to annihilation. A possible objection is raised: what if the soul were composed, avoiding contradiction? The response distinguishes between the potentiality for existence (which is in bodily matter) and the case under discussion (the soul's own potentiality for annihilation). Since the soul is simple with no material substrate, the conclusion stands: it remains after bodily separation.

Key Terms
النفس الناطقة
الفناء
البساطة
الوجود بالفعل
القوة
القابل
المقبول

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Simplicity means the soul has no parts; thus it cannot have any attributes at all.
Correction: Simplicity means no composition of parts, but the soul still has attributes; the argument denies that it can have two contradictory attributes (actuality and potentiality for annihilation) simultaneously.

Checkpoint
What is the main reason the philosophers give for the rational soul not accepting annihilation?
A) It is created by God
B) It is simple and exists in actuality
C) It is material
D) It has both actuality and potentiality

Key Takeaway
The rational soul's simplicity and actual existence entail its immortality, as it cannot be receptive to its own annihilation.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your rational soul is simple and cannot be annihilated, then what you do with it in this life determines your eternal state. Are you nurturing your soul's virtues or vices? What specific virtue will you consciously practice today, knowing that your soul's character will endure forever?
?
The philosophers argue that the soul's existence is actual and cannot accept annihilation. Yet you live as if your body is all that matters. When you face death, will you be ready to meet the permanence of your soul? What have you done today that will survive your body?
?
Consider that the soul's simplicity means it cannot have two contradictory attributes. Is your soul consistent in its orientation toward truth? Or are you living a contradiction—believing in the afterlife but acting as if this world is eternal? What one contradiction will you resolve this week?

Objection and Initial Response on the Soul's Incorruptibility
Objection and start of response to soul's incorruptibility argument

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the key objection to the argument for the soul's incorruptibility and the beginning of the response that distinguishes between mental and external existence.

Core Idea
Accepting annihilation does not require the subject to survive; it can simply mean ceasing to exist.

In this passage, the author presents an objection to the previous argument that the soul must be incorruptible. The objector challenges the claim that the recipient of annihilation must continue to exist. Instead, they argue that 'accepting annihilation' means the thing ceases to exist externally, though it can be conceived mentally. The response begins to clarify the difference between the soul and material substances.

Guided Path
1
Read the objection carefully
Read the Arabic passage and its translation, focusing on the objector's challenge to the necessity of the recipient's survival.
Notice how the objector redefines 'accepting annihilation'.
2
Understand the mental vs external distinction
The objector allows coexistence in the mind but not in external reality. Grasp why this avoids contradiction.
This distinction is key to the objection.
3
Note the beginning of the response
The author starts to differentiate the soul from other things that are 'loci'. The passage cuts off, so consider what might follow.
The response is incomplete; further lessons will complete it.

قوله : ( لأن القائل يجب بقاؤه مع حصول المقبول) يرد عليه أنا لا نسلم الوجوب فإنه ليس معنى قبول الشيء للفناء والفساد أن ذلك الشيء يبقى متحققاً ويحل فيه الفناء والفساد على قياس قبول الجسم للإعراض الحالة فيه بل معناه أن ذلك الشيء ينعدم في الخارج بطريان الفساد والفناء نعم يجوز الاجتماع في الذهن بمعنى أنه يجوز أن يحصل الشيء في الذهن ويتصور العدم الخارجي قائماً به لكن لا يلزم منه اجتماع المتنافيين. قوله : (بخلاف ما نحن فيه) تحقيق الفرق على ما حرر بعض الأذكياء إن كون الشيء محلاً
His saying: 'Because the recipient must continue to exist while the received occurs' — it is objected to that we do not accept the necessity, for the meaning of a thing's acceptance of annihilation and corruption is not that the thing remains actual and annihilation and corruption settle into it, in analogy to the body's acceptance of accidents. Rather, its meaning is that the thing ceases to exist in the external world by the occurrence of corruption and annihilation. Yes, it is possible for them to coexist in the mind, meaning that the thing can occur in the mind and one can conceive of external non-existence as inhering in it, but this does not entail the conjunction of the two contradictory things. His saying: 'Unlike the case at hand' — the verification of the difference, according to what some intelligent people have written, is that a thing being a locus...
Source pages p. 325

Commentary
This passage records an objection to the previous argument that the recipient must survive the arrival of what it receives. The objector argues that accepting annihilation does not mean the thing remains; rather, it means it ceases to exist. The coexistence of actuality and potentiality for annihilation can be mental, not real. The response begins to clarify the difference between the soul and bodily substances by noting that the potentiality for existence resides in bodily matter. The text cuts off before completing the response.

Correction: The objector shows that 'accepting annihilation' can simply mean the thing ceases to exist externally, without requiring the subject to survive.

The objection reframes 'accepting annihilation' as external cessation, not internal coexistence, challenging the need for the soul to survive.

Classification of Souls After Death: Ignorant and Wise, with Ibn Sina on the Knowledge for Eternal Happiness
Classification of souls after death with Ibn Sina's description of knowledge for eternal happiness.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how souls are classified after death into ignorant and wise, and what conditions lead to suffering or happiness, including Ibn Sina's outline of the knowledge required for eternal happiness.

Core Idea
The after-death experience of the soul depends on its knowledge and acquired dispositions.

This passage from al-Mawaqif provides a detailed classification of souls after death. It distinguishes between the ignorant and the knowledgeable, and further explains how even a knowledgeable soul may suffer if it has acquired bad habits. The author then quotes Ibn Sina, who outlines the kind of knowledge that leads to eternal happiness. This lesson will guide you through this classification and its implications.

Guided Path
1
Understand the overall classification
Read the passage and identify the two main categories: the ignorant soul and the knowledgeable soul. Notice that the ignorant soul suffers eternally, while the knowledgeable soul may or may not suffer depending on its dispositions.
The passage uses 'جاهلة' and 'عالمة'.
2
Examine the ignorant soul
Focus on why the ignorant soul suffers after death. The passage explains that it becomes aware of its deficiency with no hope of removal, while before death it was absorbed in sensory pleasures and false beliefs.
Compound ignorance (جهلاً مركباً) is key: the soul thinks it knows but is actually ignorant.
3
Analyze the knowledgeable soul
See how the knowledgeable soul is divided into those with evil dispositions (acquired from the body) and those without. Those with evil dispositions suffer from unfulfilled desires. Then read Ibn Sina's description of the knowledge that leads to eternal happiness.
Ibn Sina lists: true conception of separate principles, certain affirmation, knowledge of universal causes, understanding cosmic order, providence, and the First Essence.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنها إما جاهلة جهلاً مركباً وإما عالمة، أما الجاهلة فتتألم بعد المفارقة أبداً كالكفار عندنا وذلك لشعورها بنقصانها نقصاناً لا مطمع لها في زواله وإنما لم تتألم قبل المفارقة لأنها لما كانت مشتغلة بالمحسوسات منغمسة في العلائق البدنية ولم تكن تعقلاتها صافية عن الشوائب العادية والظنون والأوهام الكاذبة لم تتنبه لنقصانها وفوت كمالاتها؛ بل ربما تخيلت أضداد الكمال كمالاً وفرحت بعقائدها الباطلة واشتاقت الوصول إلى معتقداتها وإذا فارقت صفت تعقلاتها وشعرت بفوت كمالاتها وامتناع نيلها وحصول نقصاناتها شعوراً لا يخفى فيه التباس. وأما العالمة فأما أن تكون لها هيئات رديئة اكتسبتها بملابسة البدن ومباشرة الرذائل المقتضاة للطبيعة وميلها إلى الشهوات أولا، فإن كانت تلك الهيئات حاصلة لها تألمت بها تألماً عظيماً واشتاقت إلى مشتهياتها التي ألفت بها اشتياق العاشق المهجور الذي لم ... قوله : ( وأما العالمة إلخ قال ابن سينا في بعض كتبه أما قدر العلم الذي يحصل به السعادة الأخروية فليس يمكن أن أنص عليه نصاً إلا بالتقريب وأظن أن ذلك أن يتصور الإنسان المبادئ المفارقة تصوراً حقيقاً ويصدق بها تصديقاً يقينياً برهانياً ويعرف العلل الثانية للحركات الكلية دون الجزئية، التي لا تتناهى ويتقرر عنده هيئة الكل ونسب أجزائه بعضها إلى بعض، والنظام الآخذ من المبدأ الأول إلى أقصى الموجودات الواقعة في ترتيبه ويتصور العناية وكيفيتها ويتحقق أن الذات المتقدمة على الكل أي وجود يخصها وآية واحدة تخصها وأنها كيف يعرف
Then it (the soul) is either ignorant with compound ignorance or knowledgeable. As for the ignorant, it suffers after separation forever, like the disbelievers in our view, and that is because of its awareness of its deficiency, a deficiency with no hope of its removal. And it did not suffer before separation because, being occupied with sensory things and immersed in bodily attachments, and its intellections not being free from customary impurities and false opinions and illusions, it did not become aware of its deficiency and the loss of its perfections; rather, it perhaps imagined the opposites of perfection as perfection, rejoiced in its false beliefs, and longed to attain its objects of belief. And when it separates, its intellections become clear and it becomes aware of the loss of its perfections, the impossibility of attaining them, and the presence of its deficiencies, an awareness in which there is no concealment. As for the knowledgeable soul, it either has evil dispositions it acquired through contact with the body and direct practice of the vices required by nature and its inclination toward pleasures, or not. If those dispositions exist in it, it suffers from them a great suffering and longs for its desires to which it was accustomed, like the longing of an abandoned lover who did not... His statement: (And as for the knowledgeable, etc.) Ibn Sina said in some of his books: 'As for the amount of knowledge by which eternal happiness is attained, it is not possible for me to state it explicitly except approximately. I think that it is that a person conceives of the separate principles with a true conception, affirms them with a certain, demonstrative affirmation, knows the secondary causes of the universal motions (not the particular ones, which are infinite), and has in his mind the form of the whole, the relations of its parts to each other, and the order proceeding from the First Principle to the farthest existents in its arrangement, and conceives of providence and its modality, and ascertains that the Essence preceding the whole has an existence specific to it and a single mark specific to it, and how it is known.'
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage classifies the experience of the soul after death. The soul, being immortal, either suffers eternal pain if it is ignorant (with compound ignorance) or suffers pain if it is wise but has acquired bad habits. The ignorant soul did not feel its deficiency before death because it was absorbed in sensory pleasures and false beliefs; after separation, it becomes aware of its loss. The wise soul may have evil dispositions from the body and thus suffers from unfulfilled desires. The author then quotes Ibn Sina, who describes the knowledge that leads to eternal happiness: it involves a genuine intellectual comprehension of the separate principles, certain belief, knowledge of the universal causes, and understanding of the order of the cosmos from the First Principle.

Key Terms
الجاهلة
العالمة
الهيئات الرديئة
السعادة الأخروية
المبادئ المفارقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All souls suffer equally after death regardless of their state.
Correction: The passage distinguishes between the ignorant and the knowledgeable; the ignorant suffer eternally, while the knowledgeable may suffer only if they have evil dispositions, and through proper knowledge can attain happiness.

Checkpoint
According to the classification in this passage, why does the ignorant soul suffer after death?
A) Because of physical pain
B) Because it becomes aware of its deficiency and the impossibility of attaining perfection
C) Because it misses the pleasures of the world
D) Because it is separated from God

Key Takeaway
The state of the soul after death is determined by its knowledge and acquired dispositions; the ignorant suffer eternally, while the knowledgeable may suffer if they have bad habits, but can attain happiness through true knowledge of the metaphysical principles.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
The ignorant soul did not feel its deficiency because it was distracted by sensory pleasures. What are the 'sensory distractions' in your life that keep you from perceiving your spiritual deficiencies? Are you willing to put them aside today to seek true knowledge?
?
Ibn Sina describes a knowledge that leads to eternal happiness – a deep intellectual understanding of the order of existence. Do you have a mere belief in God, or do you strive for a demonstrative certainty? What step will you take this week to deepen your understanding of the First Principle?
?
The knowledgeable soul with evil dispositions suffers after death because of unfulfilled attachments. What attachments or habits are you nurturing that might cause you suffering when you leave this world? How can you begin to break them now?

The Suffering of Ignorant Souls After Death
Explains why ignorant souls suffer eternally after bodily separation.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand why souls with compound ignorance suffer eternally after death and why they did not suffer before death.

Core Idea
Ignorant souls suffer eternally from the awareness of their own deficiency after death.

This passage from Sharh al-Mawaqif explains the state of ignorant souls after bodily separation. Before diving into the text, consider how our attachments and false beliefs can veil us from our true state. After death, the veil lifts, and the soul perceives its loss clearly. Let's examine why this leads to eternal suffering.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own rhythm come through before you interpret.
Notice the author uses parentheses to highlight key phrases.
2
Identify the two categories of souls
The passage begins by classifying souls as either ignorant with compound ignorance or knowing. Focus on the description of the ignorant souls.
3
Understand the reason for suffering after separation
The passage states that ignorant souls suffer eternally after separation because they become aware of their deficiency and have no hope of removing it. Ask yourself why this awareness is so painful.
This is the core argument of the passage.
4
Understand why they did not suffer before separation
The passage explains that before death, they were preoccupied with sensory things and false beliefs, so they did not realize their deficiency. After death, their intellects become pure, and they see clearly.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ثم إنها إما جاهلة جهلاً مركباً ( وإما ،عالمة، أما الجاهلة فتتألم بعد المفارقة أبداً) كالكفار عندنا ( وذلك لشعورها بنقصانها نقصاناً لا مطمع لها في زواله ) وإنما لم تتألم قبل المفارقة لأنها لما كانت مشتغلة بالمحسوسات منغمسة في العلائق البدنية ولم تكن تعقلاتها صافية عن الشوائب العادية والظنون والأوهام الكاذبة لم تتنبه لنقصانها وفوت كمالاتها؛ بل ربما تخيلت أضداد الكمال كمالاً وفرحت بعقائدها الباطلة واشتاقت الوصول إلى معتقداتها وإذا فارقت صفت تعقلاتها وشعرت بفوت کمالاتها وامتناع نيلها وحصول نقصاناتها شعوراً لا يخفى فيه التباس.
Then they [the souls] are either ignorant with compound ignorance or knowing. As for the ignorant, they suffer after separation forever – like the disbelievers according to us – and that is because of their awareness of their deficiency, a deficiency for which they have no hope of removal. They did not suffer before separation because, since they were occupied with sensory things, immersed in bodily attachments, and their intellects were not pure from ordinary impurities, false opinions, and deceptive fancies, they were not alerted to their deficiency and the loss of their perfections; rather, they perhaps imagined the opposites of perfection as perfection and rejoiced in their false beliefs and yearned to attain their objects of belief. When they separate, their intellects become pure and they become aware of the loss of their perfections, the impossibility of attaining them, and the occurrence of their deficiencies, an awareness in which there is no confusion.
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage describes the state of ignorant souls after death, specifically those with compound ignorance (الجهل المركب). It states that they suffer eternally after separation from the body because they become aware of their own deficiency and realize they can never attain perfection. The author explains why they did not suffer before death: they were preoccupied with sensory pleasures, immersed in bodily attachments, and their intellects were clouded by false fancies and opinions. They even imagined that their false beliefs were perfections and rejoiced in them. After death, their intellects are purified, and they clearly perceive their loss. This explains the eternal punishment of disbelievers according to Islamic theology.

جهل مركب
مفارقة

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: Some might think the suffering of ignorant souls is due to external punishment.
Correction: The passage emphasizes that the suffering is due to internal awareness of deficiency, not external punishment.

B) They are rewarded in this life for their ignorance.
C) They have no soul until after death.
D) They will be reborn and suffer later.

Key Takeaway
Ignorant souls suffer eternally after death because they become aware of their irremediable deficiency.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
Do you live as if you are already aware of your ultimate deficiency, or do you, like the ignorant soul, immerse yourself in worldly attachments to avoid that awareness? What will happen on that day when your intellect is purified and you can no longer hide from the truth? Answer honestly: are you preparing your soul for that inevitable awareness?
?
The ignorant soul rejoiced in its false beliefs and yearned for them. Are you absolutely certain that what you hold as truth is not a form of compound ignorance? How would you know? Are you willing to face the possibility that your deepest convictions might be illusions that will turn into eternal regret?
?
The soul did not suffer before death because it was distracted by sensory pleasures and false opinions. What distractions in your own life are currently shielding you from recognizing your spiritual deficiency? What would it take for you to honestly confront your shortcomings now, before it is too late?

Afterlife States of Knowing and Simple Souls
Classifies knowing souls and simple souls regarding their afterlife suffering or bliss.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how knowing souls and simple souls are classified in the afterlife based on their moral and intellectual state, including the role of bad dispositions and awareness of perfection.

Core Idea
Knowledge alone doesn't guarantee bliss—purification matters.

This passage from the commentary on al-Mawaqif presents a nuanced classification of souls in the afterlife. Drawing on Ibn Sina, it distinguishes between knowing souls that have acquired bad dispositions and those that are pure, as well as simple souls without knowledge. The key insight is that even among the knowing, punishment can be temporary if they have faith but also bad habits.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage and translation
Read the Arabic passage once without analysis, then the translation. Notice the dense philosophical style of Ibn Sina's quote and the commentary's classification.
2
Identify Ibn Sina's knowledge requirements
Focus on the portion from 'أما قدر العلم' to 'كيف يعرف'. This describes the approximate knowledge needed for afterlife felicity. List the key components mentioned.
The passage is a commentary on 'وأما العالمة'.
3
Distinguish the two types of knowing souls
The passage presents two cases: (1) souls with bad dispositions (هيئات رديئة) that suffer but eventually have punishment cut off, like the sinful believer; (2) souls without bad dispositions, perfect and pure, enjoying eternal bliss, like the pious believer.
Note the role of 'ركون إلى البدن' and 'محبتها للبدن' in acquiring dispositions.
4
Understand the simple souls category
Simple souls (النفوس الساذجة) are those with sound hearts and little worldly concern. They are not punished because they lack awareness of perfections and longing for them, like non-mukallaf individuals.
This shows that punishment requires some degree of knowledge or responsibility.
5
Reflect on the theological implications
Consider how this classification aligns with Islamic views on the sinful believer (ultimately purified) and the fate of children or the insane. Note that this passage is from a philosophical-theological commentary.
The mu'min fasiq and mu'min muttaqi are examples from our tradition.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قوله : ( وأما العالمة إلخ قال ابن سينا في بعض كتبه أما قدر العلم الذي يحصل به السعادة الأخروية فليس يمكن أن أنص عليه نصاً إلا بالتقريب وأظن أن ذلك أن يتصور الإنسان المبادئ المفارقة تصوراً حقيقاً ويصدق بها تصديقاً يقينياً برهانياً ويعرف العلل الثانية للحركات الكلية دون الجزئية، التي لا تتناهى ويتقرر عنده هيئة الكل ونسب أجزائه بعضها إلى بعض، والنظام الآخذ من المبدأ الأول إلى أقصى الموجودات الواقعة في ترتيبه ويتصور العناية وكيفيتها ويتحقق أن الذات المتقدمة على الكل أي وجود يخصها وآية واحدة تخصها وأنها كيف يعرف ينسي يبق له رجاء الوصول، ما دامت تلك الهيئات ( باقية فيها لكنها تزول عاقبة الأمر بحسب شدة رسوخها فيها وضعفها لأنها إنما حصلت لها للركون إلى البدن وجرتها ) أي جرت وكسبت تلك الهيئات للنفس ) محبتها له ( أي للبدن ) وذلك مما بطول العهد به وتزول بالتدريج ) وتقطع عقوبتها بها كالمؤمن الفاسق على رأينا ( وإن لم تكن تلك الهيئات للنفس بل كانت كاملة بريئة عن الهيئات الرديئة التذت بها ) أي يوجد أن لذاتها كذلك ، ( أبداً مبتهجة بإدراك كمالها ) ، ( أبداً مبتهجة بإدراك كمالها ) باقياً سرمداً كالمؤمن المتقي عندنا، وأما النفوس الساذجة التي غلبت عليها سلامة الصدور وقلة الاهتمام بأمور الدنيا فلا عقوبة لها لعدم شعورها بالكمالات وانتفاء اشتياقها إليها كغير المكلفين عندنا
His statement: 'And as for the knowing [souls], etc.' Ibn Sina said in one of his books: 'As for the amount of knowledge by which the felicity of the afterlife is obtained, it is not possible for me to state it explicitly except by approximation. I think that it is that a person should truly conceive the separate principles, affirm them with certain demonstrative assent, know the secondary causes of the universal motions (not the particular infinite ones), have established in his mind the form of the whole and the proportions of its parts to each other, and the order proceeding from the First Principle to the furthest existents occurring in its arrangement, and conceive providence and its modality, and realize that the Essence prior to the whole has its own existence and a unique sign, and how It knows... He retains hope of attainment as long as those [bad] dispositions remain in it, but they disappear eventually according to the intensity of their rootedness or weakness, because they are acquired through attachment to the body, and love of it draws those dispositions to the soul. That disappears over long time and gradually, and its punishment is cut off with it, like the sinful believer according to our view. And if those dispositions are not present in the soul, but it is perfect and free from base dispositions, it delights in them – that is, its pleasures are such – eternally, rejoicing in the perception of its perfection, remaining forever, like the pious believer according to us. As for the simple souls that are dominated by soundness of heart and lack of concern for worldly matters, there is no punishment for them due to their lack of awareness of perfections and absence of longing for them, like non-mukallaf persons according to us.'
Source pages p. 326

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on the phrase 'وأما العالمة' (as for the knowing souls). It first quotes Ibn Sina's description of the knowledge required for afterlife felicity: true conception of separate principles, certain belief, understanding of universal causes, the order of the universe from the First Principle, and providence. Then the commentary explains the two cases of knowing souls: those who have acquired bad dispositions (هيئات رديئة) suffer great pain and longing like a forsaken lover, but their punishment eventually ends if they are sinners believers (المؤمن الفاسق) because the dispositions weaken over time. The second case: those without bad dispositions, who are perfect and pure, enjoy eternal bliss, rejoicing in their perfection, like the pious believer (المؤمن المتقي). Finally, it mentions simple souls (النفوس الساذجة) who are pure-hearted and indifferent to worldly matters; they have no punishment because they lack awareness of perfections and longing for them, like non-mukallaf persons (children, etc.).

Key Terms
عالمة
هيئات رديئة
مشتاق
عقوبة
كامل
نفوس ساذجة
مؤمن فاسق
مؤمن متقي

Key Terms from Source Passage

Misconception & Correction
Misconception: All knowing souls are automatically saved and experience eternal bliss.
Correction: Knowledge alone does not guarantee bliss; souls that have acquired bad dispositions through attachment to the body suffer temporarily until those dispositions fade, like the sinful believer.

Checkpoint
According to the passage, what happens to a knowing soul that has acquired bad dispositions?
A) It suffers eternally without end.
B) It suffers until the dispositions disappear gradually, then its punishment ends, like the sinful believer.
C) It immediately becomes pure and rejoices.
D) It transmigrates to another body.

Key Takeaway
The afterlife state of a soul depends on its moral purification and awareness, not just on knowledge; simple souls are not punished due to lack of awareness, while knowing souls with bad dispositions experience temporary suffering.

How This Lesson Applies — to MY Life?
?
If your soul were examined today, would it be more like the knowing soul with bad dispositions or the simple soul? What specific attachments are you holding onto that might cause temporary suffering after death?
?
You have read that even knowing souls can be burdened by base dispositions. Which of your habits or attachments are you most afraid to face in the afterlife, and what are you willing to uproot starting now?
?
The simple souls are not punished because they lacked awareness. But you now have awareness. What will you do with this knowledge so that you do not become like the knowing soul that only suffers?

The Philosophers' Classification of Souls in the Afterlife
Reports the majority of philosophers' view on spiritual resurrection of perfected souls.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand the philosophers' classification of souls (perfect, imperfect, simple) and their corresponding states in the afterlife as presented in this passage.

Core Idea
The soul's fate depends on its purification and attachment to the body.

This passage presents the philosophers' detailed view on the spiritual resurrection of souls. It distinguishes three types of souls and explains their respective experiences after death. As you read, notice how the philosophers align their view with certain categories familiar from Islamic theology.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage carefully
Read the Arabic passage once without trying to analyse it. Then read the translation. Let the author's own classification come through.
Notice the three types of souls mentioned: those with base dispositions, perfect souls, and simple souls.
2
Identify the three categories and their outcomes
Find each type of soul and the corresponding afterlife state: punishment for imperfect, bliss for perfect, and no punishment for simple souls.
The passage uses examples like the sinful believer and the righteous believer to illustrate.
3
Reflect on the comparison with Islamic views
The passage explicitly compares the philosophers' view to Islamic concepts. Think about how this shapes the discussion of bodily resurrection.

Source Text

المرصد الثاني - المقصد الثالث : في إنكار حشر الأجساد

The second observation - the third objective: On the denial of the resurrection of bodies. He says: There remains for him hope of attainment, as long as those dispositions remain in it, but they vanish in the end according to the intensity and weakness of their rootedness in it, because they occurred to it due to its leaning towards the body. Its love for it drew those dispositions to the soul, and that is something that takes a long time and passes away gradually. Its punishment by them is cut off like the sinful believer according to our view. And if those dispositions were not for the soul, but rather it was perfect and innocent of base dispositions, it delights in them eternally rejoicing in the perception of its perfection, remaining forever like the righteous believer according to us. As for simple souls that are dominated by soundness of heart and lack of concern for worldly matters, there is no punishment for them due to their lack of awareness of perfections and absence of yearning for them, like the non-accountable according to us. This is what the majority of them hold.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage defines the philosophers' view on the afterlife of the soul. It begins by explaining that for imperfect souls, attachment to bodily habits causes gradual punishment until they are purified, using the example of the sinful believer. For perfect souls, they attain eternal bliss. Simple souls (like children or the non-accountable) have no punishment due to lack of awareness. The passage concludes that this is the view of the majority of them (i.e., philosophers). The structure follows a classification of souls and their respective afterlives.

Imam al-Razi's Reconciliation of Spiritual and Bodily Resurrection
Al-Razi's argument for reconciling spiritual and bodily resurrection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will understand how Imam al-Razi reconciles spiritual and bodily resurrection by explaining that the soul's weakness prevents combination in this life, but after death and purification, it can attain both in the afterlife.

Core Idea
Al-Razi: The soul's weakness in this world is the obstacle; after death, it can enjoy both spiritual and bodily pleasures.

Imam al-Razi addresses a key theological question: Can the afterlife include both spiritual and physical pleasures? He argues that while they are incompatible in this life due to the soul's limitations, the soul becomes capable of combining them after death. This lesson unpacks his reasoning.

Guided Path
1
Read the passage
Begin by reading the Arabic text and its English translation to get an overview of al-Razi's argument.
Pay attention to the distinction between spiritual and bodily happiness.
2
Identify the two types of happiness
Notice that al-Razi describes spiritual happiness as knowledge and love of God, and bodily happiness as sensory pleasures.
These are distinct and seemingly mutually exclusive in this life.
3
Understand the problem and the solution
Al-Razi explains that in this life, the soul is too weak to attend to both. But after death, it draws from the spiritual world, becomes strong, and when re-embodied, can experience both.
The key is the purification after death.
4
Reflect on the conclusion
Al-Razi calls this the ultimate goal of happiness. Consider how this reconciles reason and revelation.
This shows the harmony between philosophy and divine law.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
قال الإمام الرازي: وأما القائلون بالمعاد الروحاني والجسماني معاً فقد أرادوا أن يجمعوا بين الحكمة والشريعة، فقالوا : دل العقل على أن سعادة الأرواح بمعرفة الله تعالى ،ومحبته وأن سعادة الأجسام في إدراك المحسوسات والجمع بين هاتين السعادتين في هذه الحياة غير ممكن لأن الإنسان
حتى لا يلحقها تكثر وتغير بوجه من الوجوه وكيف نسبة ترتب الموجودات إليها ثم كلما ازداد النظر استبصاراً ازداد للسعادة استعداداً .
مع استغراقه في تجلي أنوار عالم الغيب لا يمكنه الالتفات إلى شيء من الجسمانية، ومع استقرائه في استيفاء هذه اللذات لا يمكنه أن يلتفت إلى اللذات الروحانية، وإما تعذر هذا الجمع لكون الأرواح البشرية ضعيفة في هذا العالم، فإذا فارقت بالموت واستمدت من عالم القدس والطهارة قويت وكملت، فإذا أعيدت إلى الأبدان مرة ثانية كانت قوية قادرة على الجمع بين الأمرين ولا شبهة في أن هذه الغاية القصوى من مراتب السعادات
Imam al-Razi said: As for those who affirm both spiritual and bodily resurrection, they intended to combine wisdom and divine law. They said: Reason indicates that the happiness of souls is through knowledge of Allah Almighty and love of Him, and that the happiness of bodies is through the perception of sensible things. The combination of these two happinesses in this life is impossible because the human being, even if they are not affected by multiplicity and change in any way, and how is the rank ordering of existents? Then the more one increases in insight, the more one increases in preparation for happiness. While immersed in the manifestation of the lights of the unseen world, one cannot pay attention to anything physical; and while engrossed in fully experiencing these pleasures, one cannot turn to spiritual pleasures. This combination is impossible due to the human souls being weak in this world. So when they separate by death and derive from the world of sanctity and purity, they become strong and complete. Then when they are returned to bodies a second time, they are strong, capable of combining both matters. There is no doubt that this is the ultimate goal among the ranks of happiness.
Source pages p. 327

Commentary
This passage presents Imam al-Razi's reconciliation of spiritual and bodily resurrection. It begins by stating that those who affirm both resurrection types aim to harmonize philosophy and revelation. Reason shows that the happiness of souls is in knowing and loving God, while bodily happiness is in sensory perception. In this life, it is impossible to combine both because the soul, when immersed in spiritual insight, cannot attend to physical pleasures, and vice versa. The weakness of human souls in this world prevents the combination. However, after death, when the soul leaves the body and draws from the spiritual world, it becomes strong and perfected. When it is returned to a body in the resurrection, it is capable of enjoying both spiritual and bodily pleasures simultaneously, which is the highest rank of happiness.

Two proofs for the current creation of Paradise and Hell with objections.

Core Idea
Paradise and Hell exist now, as shown by Adam's story and Quranic past tense.

This lesson presents the fourth objective of the second observation, where the author defends the Ash'ari position that Paradise and Hell are created now, against the Mu'tazila who say they will be created on Judgment Day. Two proofs are advanced, each followed by an objection. The author's response to the second objection is incomplete in this passage.

Read the passage

The objector claims the garden of Adam was a worldly garden, but the author responds that this contradicts consensus and the definite article.
